Temporal Origination: Preceded by Non-Existence
Definition of temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of temporal origination as being preceded by non-existence and having a first moment of existence.

Core Idea
Temporal origination means something begins to exist after not existing.

In kalam, the term 'originated' (hadith) can refer to things that come into existence after being non-existent. The commentator first defines this as temporal origination, linking it to a first moment in time. This lesson unpacks that definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the first definition
Read the Arabic sentence defining the originated (al-hadith) as preceded by non-existence. Follow the translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the emphasis on the first moment of existence.
2
Understand 'preceded by non-existence'
Reflect on what it means for something's non-existence to be before its existence. This implies a temporal prior.
3
Note the contrast with temporal eternal
The text mentions the term 'temporal eternal' (al-qadim al-zamani) as the opposite. Understand that temporal eternal has no beginning.
This contrast helps clarify the definition.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أبحاث الحدوث وهي أيضاً راجعة إلى أمرين أحدهما أن الحادث هو المسبوق بالعدم أي يكون عدمه قبل وجوده فيكون له أي لوجوده أول هو أي الحادث معدوم قبله أي قبل ذلك الأول، وهذا هو المسمى بالحادث الزماني ويقابله القديم الزماني . وقيل : هو المسبوق بالغير سبقاً ذاتياً، سواء كان هناك سبق زماني أو لا.
In the investigations of origination, and they also pertain to two matters: one of them is that the originated (al-hadith) is that which is preceded by non-existence, meaning its non-existence is before its existence, so its existence has a first moment, and it is non-existent before that first moment. This is what is called temporal originated (al-hadith al-zamani), and it is contrasted with the temporal eternal (al-qadim al-zamani). And it is said: it is that which is preceded by another in essential precedence (al-sabq al-dhati), whether there is temporal precedence or not.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'origination' (al-haduth) as understood in kalam. The commentator states that the investigations of origination pertain to two matters. The first matter is the definition of the originated as that which is preceded by non-existence, i.e., its non-existence comes before its existence. This means that its existence has a first moment, and before that moment it was non-existent. This is called temporal origination (al-hadith al-zamani), and it is contrasted with the temporal eternal (al-qadim al-zamani). The second definition, introduced by 'and it is said', defines the originated as that which is preceded by another in essential precedence (al-sabq al-dhati), regardless of whether there is temporal precedence or not. This broader definition encompasses both temporal and essential origination. The passage sets up the two key types of origination discussed in the text.

Key Terms
حادث
حدوث
مسبوق بالعدم
قديم زماني
سبق زماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Origination always means temporal origination.
Correction: The text introduces a second definition later, so temporal is not the only meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first definition of al-hadith?
A) That which is preceded by another in essential precedence.
B) That which is preceded by non-existence, so its existence has a first moment.
C) That which is eternal in time.
D) That which is not preceded by anything.

Key Takeaway
Temporal origination is defined as existence preceded by non-existence with a first moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a temporal originated being — you had a first moment. Does your daily life reflect the reality that you were once nothing and that your entire existence is a gift? What would change if you truly lived with this awareness every hour?
?
If everything originated has a beginning, then your Creator alone has no beginning. How does that contrast make you feel about your dependence on Him? In what specific area of your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient rather than utterly dependent?
?
You learned that 'originated' means preceded by non-existence. When you look at the world of pleasures and distractions, do you treat them as if they are eternal? What would it mean for you to live today with the certain knowledge that everything temporal will end?

Essential Origination: Preceded by Another in Essential Priority
Definition of essential origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the broader definition of origination as being preceded by another in essential precedence, which includes both temporal and non-temporal cases.

Core Idea
Essential origination means depending on something else for existence, not just temporal beginning.

The commentator introduces a second, more general definition of origination: being preceded by another in essential precedence. This expands the concept beyond time, allowing for things that are eternal but still dependent. This lesson explains this key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the second definition
Read the Arabic sentence stating that originated is that which is preceded by another in essential precedence. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'sabq dhati' – essential precedence.
2
Understand 'essential precedence'
Essential precedence means one thing is prior to another in essence, not necessarily in time. For example, the cause is essentially prior to the effect.
3
Note the inclusive condition
The definition is explicitly broader: 'whether there is temporal precedence or not.' This means it covers both temporal and essential origination.
This is the key difference from the first definition.
4
Contrast with temporal origination
Compare this definition with the first one. Understand that essential origination does not require a temporal beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أبحاث الحدوث وهي أيضاً راجعة إلى أمرين أحدهما أن الحادث هو المسبوق بالعدم أي يكون عدمه قبل وجوده فيكون له أي لوجوده أول هو أي الحادث معدوم قبله أي قبل ذلك الأول، وهذا هو المسمى بالحادث الزماني ويقابله القديم الزماني . وقيل : هو المسبوق بالغير سبقاً ذاتياً، سواء كان هناك سبق زماني أو لا.
In the investigations of origination, and they also pertain to two matters: one of them is that the originated (al-hadith) is that which is preceded by non-existence, meaning its non-existence is before its existence, so its existence has a first moment, and it is non-existent before that first moment. This is what is called temporal originated (al-hadith al-zamani), and it is contrasted with the temporal eternal (al-qadim al-zamani). And it is said: it is that which is preceded by another in essential precedence (al-sabq al-dhati), whether there is temporal precedence or not.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'origination' (al-haduth) as understood in kalam. The commentator states that the investigations of origination pertain to two matters. The first matter is the definition of the originated as that which is preceded by non-existence, i.e., its non-existence comes before its existence. This means that its existence has a first moment, and before that moment it was non-existent. This is called temporal origination (al-hadith al-zamani), and it is contrasted with the temporal eternal (al-qadim al-zamani). The second definition, introduced by 'and it is said', defines the originated as that which is preceded by another in essential precedence (al-sabq al-dhati), regardless of whether there is temporal precedence or not. This broader definition encompasses both temporal and essential origination. The passage sets up the two key types of origination discussed in the text.

Key Terms
حدوث
مسبوق بالغير
سبق ذاتي
سبق زماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All originated things are temporal.
Correction: Essential origination includes non-temporal entities that depend on another, such as attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the second definition, what does 'preceded by another in essential precedence' mean?
A) It means preceded in time.
B) It means preceded in essence, regardless of time.
C) It means preceded in rank.
D) It means preceded in location.

Key Takeaway
Essential origination is a broader category defined by essential priority, not necessarily temporal priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything other than Allah is essentially originated (dependent on Him), does your daily life reflect that awareness, or do you often act as if you are self-sufficient? How will you change one habit today to acknowledge your dependency?
?
This lesson defines essential origination as being preceded by another in essential priority, not just time. Have you ever mistakenly believed that only things with a temporal beginning are dependent on Allah? How might that misconception have affected your sense of reliance on Him in areas that have always existed in your life?
?
The definition includes things that have no temporal beginning but still depend on Allah. Consider something in your life that you consider 'always there' (e.g., your abilities, relationships). Do you recognize its essential dependency on Allah every moment? Write a specific prayer acknowledging that dependency.

What Is Kalam?

Definition vs. Investigation in Kalam
Commentator clarifies definition vs. proof of origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining 'originated' is a conceptual preliminary, not an investigation, and what the first investigation actually entails.

Core Idea
Defining a term is not proving its reality.

When studying kalam, it's easy to confuse defining a term with proving it. This passage clarifies that the definition of 'originated' (al-hadith) is merely a conceptual preliminary (mabda' tasawwuri), while the real investigation begins with proving that contingent things have essential origination. Let's unpack this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to grasp the overall flow. Notice the key terms: تعريف (definition), إثبات (affirmation), بحث (investigation).
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two main points: (1) The definition of 'originated' is not an investigation. (2) The first investigation is affirming essential origination for contingent things. Find these in the text.
3
Understand why definition is not investigation
The commentator says that investigation (bahth) is about affirming predicates to subjects. A definition simply clarifies a concept, so it belongs to conceptual preliminaries (al-mabadi' al-tasawwuriyya).
This distinction prevents confusion between conceptual clarification and substantive proof.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
The commentator also allows that 'investigation' might be used in its linguistic sense, meaning 'discussion'. This flexibility shows the importance of understanding context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أحدهما أن الحادث هو المسبوق بالعدم البحث إثبات المحمولات للموضوعات أعني الذوات فتعريف الحدوث ليس من قبيل البحث، بل البحث الأول هو إثبات الحدوث الذاتي للممكنات وأما التعريف فمن قبيل المبادئ التصورية ويمكن أن يراد بالبحث المعنى اللغوي.
His saying: (one of them is that the originated is that which is preceded by non-existence. The investigation is the affirmation of predicates to subjects, i.e., essences. So the definition of origination is not of the order of investigation; rather, the first investigation is the affirmation of essential origination for contingent things. As for the definition, it is of the order of conceptual preliminaries. And it is possible that by 'investigation' the linguistic meaning is intended.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of the 'investigation' (bahth) in the context of the first definition. The commentator explains that the author's phrase 'one of them is that the originated is preceded by non-existence' is not itself an investigation but a definition. The actual investigation is the affirmation of predicates to subjects, i.e., essences. So defining origination is not a 'bahth' in the technical sense; rather, the first investigation is the proof that contingent beings have essential origination. The definition belongs to conceptual preliminaries (al-mabadi' al-tasawwuriyya). The commentator also allows that 'investigation' might be used in its linguistic sense. This lesson distinguishes between conceptual definition and real proof.

Key Terms
تعريف
إثبات
البحث
المبادئ التصورية
المعنى اللغوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining a term like 'originated' is already part of the investigation and that the definition itself is a proof.
Correction: The commentator states that the definition is a conceptual preliminary, not an investigation. The investigation is the subsequent proof that contingent things actually have that attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the first investigation (bahth) in this section?
A) The definition of al-hadith.
B) The affirmation of essential origination for contingent things.
C) The classification of temporal and essential origination.
D) The linguistic meaning of investigation.

Key Takeaway
Defining a term (like 'originated') is only a conceptual preliminary, while the real investigation is proving that contingent things actually possess that attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when have you confused knowing the definition of something (like 'originated') with truly grasping its reality in your heart? How will you move from merely defining to investigating today?
?
The commentator stresses that the real investigation is proving that contingent things are originated. Have you personally wrestled with the proof that the universe had a beginning, or do you simply accept it as a definition? What would it take for you to own that truth for yourself?

What Is Kalam?

Two Definitions of Origination: Temporal and Essential
Defines temporal and essential origination and contrasts their scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between temporal origination (al-ḥadath al-zamānī) and essential origination (al-ḥadath al-dhātī), and why essential origination is more general.

Core Idea
Origination can be defined by time or by dependence; essential origination is the broader concept.

This passage from al-Ījī's Mawāqif, explained by al-Jurjānī, lays the foundational definitions for one of the central topics in kalām: the origination of the world. Before engaging in proofs for the existence of God, theologians must clarify what it means for something to be 'originated'. The text presents two competing definitions—temporal and essential—and shows their logical relationship, a crucial step for arguments about the eternity of the world and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through.
The passage includes both the main text and commentary.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the first definition: the originated is preceded by non-existence (temporal). Find the second: the originated is preceded by another in essence (essential).
Note the key terms: al-ḥadath al-zamānī and al-ḥadath al-dhātī.
3
Understand why essential is more general
The passage says the second interpretation is more general because it includes temporally eternal effects that are caused (like divine attributes). Ask yourself how that works.
The commentator explains the logic of the comparison.
4
Review the commentator's clarifications
The commentary resolves potential confusions about the wording and the scope of the definitions. Pay attention to his explanation of 'essential precedence'.
Focus on the paragraphs explaining 'سبقاً ذاتياً' and 'سواء كان'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : أبحاث الحدوث ( في أبحاث الحدوث وهي أيضاً راجعة إلى أمرين أحدهما أن الحادث هو المسبوق بالعدم أي يكون عدمه قبل وجوده فيكون (له أي لوجوده) أول هو (أي الحادث) معدوم قبله (أي قبل ذلك الأول)، وهذا هو المسمى بالحادث الزماني ويقابله القديم الزماني . ( وقيل : هو المسبوق بالغير سبقاً ذاتياً، سواء كان هناك سبق زماني أو لا، وهو المسمى بالحادث الذاتي وبإزائه القديم الذاتي، ( فيكون ) الحادث بالتفسير الثاني (أعم) منه بالتفسير الأول؛ إذ المعلول القديم بحسب الزمان ( إن ثبت كان حادثاً بهذا المعنى الثاني لأن كل معلول مسبوق بغيره الذي هو علته سبقاً ذاتياً، دون المعنى الأول .
قوله : ( وهي أيضاً راجعة إلخ) قدر الشارح قدس سره هذا الكلام للإشارة إلى وجه التعبير أن المذكور أمران إلى وجه صحة قوله : أحدهما بضمير التثنية، على ما في أكثر النسخ، وهو أنه راجع إلى المذكور معنى أعني لفظ أمرين المفهوم من ذكر الأبحاث لكونها بالأبحاث مع راجعة إليهما .
قوله : (أي) يكون إلخ ) إِشارة إلى أن المراد مسبوقية وجوده، إذ لا مسبوقية للذات عن العدم، فالحدوث صفة للوجود في نفسه وللحادث باعتبار متعلقه وإلى أن المراد المسبوقية الزمانية، إذ قبلية العدم على الوجود زمانية .
قوله : (سبقاً ذاتياً ) بقرينة التفريع أعني قوله : فيكون الحادث أعم فإنه لو أريد السبق الزماني كان الحادث الذاتي والزماني متساويين وكذا القديمان ضرورة أن المسبوق بالغير سبقاً زمانيا يكون ذلك الغير في زمان عدمه وإنما لم يرد الأعم من الذاتي والزماني لأن الحدوث الذاتي ليس أعم من الحدوث الزماني صدقاً بل وجوداً يشير إليه قوله سواء كان هناك سبق زماني أو لا حيث لم يقل سواء كان زمانيا أو لا.
قوله : ( سواء كان إلخ) أشار بهذا التعميم إلى أن حصر الحادث على المسبوق بالغير معناه أنه ليس مقصوراً على المسبوق بالعدم لا على أنه ليس ذلك معناه .
قوله: (وهي أيضاً راجعة إلى أمرين إلخ إما إشارة إلى توجيه قول المصنف : أحدهما بتثنية الضمير على ما في أكثر النسخ ، مع أن الظاهر أحدهما لرجوعه إلى الأبحاث، وإما إشارة إلى وجه اقتصاره على بحثين مع أنه عنون المقصد بالأبحاث إن كانت العبارة أحدها على ما في بعض النسخ، ويؤيده قوله : وثانيهما أي ثاني أبحاث الحادث.
قوله : ( أحدهما أن الحادث هو المسبوق بالعدم البحث إثبات المحمولات للموضوعات أعني الذوات فتعريف الحدوث ليس من قبيل البحث، بل البحث الأول هو إثبات الحدوث الذاتي للممكنات وأما التعريف فمن قبيل المبادئ التصورية ويمكن أن يراد بالبحث المعنى اللغوي.
قوله : (أعم منه بالتفسير الأول، وكذا من القديم الزماني والقديم الذاتي أخص من القديم الزماني .
قوله: (إن ثبت) إنما قال ذلك للتردد في ثبوت الصفات القديمة، وإن ذهب إليه الجمهور.
The Sixth Purpose: Investigations of Origination. (Concerning investigations of origination, and they also revert to two matters: one of them is that the originated is that which is preceded by non-existence, i.e., its non-existence is before its existence, so for it (i.e., for its existence) there is a first, and it (i.e., the originated) is non-existent before it (i.e., before that first). This is what is called temporal origination, and its opposite is the temporal eternal. (And it is said: it is that which is preceded by another with an essential precedence, whether there is temporal precedence or not, and it is called essential origination, and its opposite is the essential eternal. So the originated according to the second interpretation is more general than according to the first interpretation; for the eternal effect in terms of time—if it is established—would be originated in this second sense because every effect is preceded by its cause with an essential precedence, unlike the first sense.) His saying: (And they also revert to etc.) The commentator, may God sanctify his secret, intended this speech to indicate the aspect of expression that the mentioned are two matters, and to indicate the correctness of his saying: 'one of them' with a dual pronoun, according to most copies, and that it refers to the meaning of the mentioned, namely the word 'two matters' understood from mentioning the investigations because they revert to them. His saying: (i.e., it is etc.) indicates that what is meant is the priority of its existence, for there is no priority of the essence over non-existence; thus origination is an attribute of existence itself and of the originated by virtue of its relation, and that what is meant is temporal priority, for the priority of non-existence over existence is temporal. His saying: (with an essential precedence) by the evidence of the branching, namely his saying: 'so the originated is more general'; for if temporal precedence were intended, the essential and temporal originated would be equivalent, and likewise the two eternals, necessarily because that which is preceded by another temporally has that other in the time of its non-existence. He did not intend the general (including both essential and temporal) because essential origination is not more general than temporal origination in actual fact, but in existence, as indicated by his saying 'whether there is temporal precedence or not' since he did not say 'whether it is temporal or not'. His saying: (whether there is etc.) He indicated by this generalization that restricting the originated to that preceded by another means that it is not confined to that preceded by non-existence, not that this is not its meaning. His saying: (And they also revert to two matters etc.) Either an indication to justify the author's saying: 'one of them' with a dual pronoun according to most copies, although the apparent would be 'one of them' (singular) because it refers to the investigations, or an indication to the reason for his confining himself to two investigations although he titled the purpose as 'investigations' if the expression is 'one of them' according to some copies, and his saying: 'and the second of them'—i.e., the second of the investigations of the originated—supports it. His saying: (One of them is that the originated is preceded by non-existence—the investigation is the affirmation of predicates for subjects, namely essences; so the definition of origination is not of the kind of investigation; rather, the first investigation is the affirmation of essential origination for possibles; as for the definition, it is of the kind of conceptual principles; and it is possible that by 'investigation' the literal meaning is intended. His saying: (more general than according to the first interpretation, and likewise from the temporal eternal, and the essential eternal is more specific than the temporal eternal. His saying: (if it is established) He only said that due to hesitation about the establishment of eternal attributes, although the majority held it.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage defines two types of origination (ḥudūth) and explains their relationship. It begins by stating that the investigations of origination revert to two matters. The first matter defines the originated (al-ḥādith) as that which is preceded by non-existence (al-masbūq bi'l-ʿadam)—its non-existence is before its existence. This is called temporal origination (al-ḥadath al-zamānī), and its opposite is the temporal eternal (al-qadīm al-zamānī). The second definition defines it as that which is preceded by another with an essential precedence (al-sabq al-dhātī), regardless of temporal precedence. This is called essential origination (al-ḥadath al-dhātī), and its opposite is the essential eternal (al-qadīm al-dhātī). The commentator explains that the second definition is more general (aʿamm) than the first because the eternal effect in time, if established (e.g., the attributes of God according to some), would be originated in the second sense but not the first. The commentary then clarifies the wording of the original text: the use of the dual pronoun 'one of them' (aḥaduhumā) refers to the two matters, and the definition of origination is not itself an investigation but a conceptual preliminary. The commentator also explains that the phrase 'with an essential precedence' indicates that essential origination is not more general in terms of actual occurrence (ṣidqan) but in terms of existence (wujūdan), and that the generalization 'whether there is temporal precedence or not' shows that the second definition is not confined to non-existence precedence. Finally, the commentator notes that the author's expression 'if it is established' reflects hesitation about the eternity of attributes, though the majority holds that view.

Key Terms
الحدوث الزماني
الحدوث الذاتي
القديم الزماني
القديم الذاتي
المسبوق بالعدم
المسبوق بالغير
سبق ذاتي
سبق زماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That essential origination is simply a different term for temporal origination.
Correction: Essential origination is more general; it includes things that are temporally eternal but caused (like divine attributes), so it is not equivalent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the two definitions of origination is more general?
A) Temporal origination
B) Essential origination
C) Both are equally general
D) Neither is general

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes two definitions of origination and shows that essential origination (being preceded by a cause) is more comprehensive than temporal origination (being preceded by non-existence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that everything besides Allah is 'originated'—either in time or by dependence? Does that include your own existence? How does that affect your sense of self-sufficiency? Will you today reflect on your absolute need for Allah in every moment?
?
The passage distinguishes between being preceded by non-existence and being preceded by a cause. Which type of origination do you think best describes your own relationship to Allah? Are you aware that you are constantly dependent on Him, not just at the moment of your birth? What would it take for you to live with that awareness?
?
If essential origination is more general, then even things that have always existed (like the divine attributes in the Ash'ari view) are still originated in a sense. What does that tell you about the uniqueness of Allah's eternity? How does that challenge any assumption you might have that something other than Allah could be eternal in any way?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
Essential Origination: Definition and Scope
Defines huduth dhati and distinguishes it from temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of essential origination (al-huduth al-dhati) and how it differs from temporal origination.

Core Idea
Origination can be essential, not just temporal. Causedness implies priority in essence.

This passage from al-Iji's Mawāqif introduces a key distinction within the concept of origination (ḥudūth). While common understanding equates 'originated' (ḥādith) with having a temporal beginning, Ashʿarī theology recognizes a second type: essential origination, which applies even to eternal things that depend on a cause. This subtle division is crucial for debates about the world's eternity versus origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the two interpretations of 'originated'.
The passage uses technical terms: ḥādith, hudūth dhatī, qadīm dhatī.
2
Identify the two types of origination.
The author distinguishes temporal origination (ḥudūth zamānī) from essential origination (ḥudūth dhātī). Which one is broader according to the passage?
The key sentence: 'فيكون الحادث بالتفسير الثاني أعم منه بالتفسير الأول'.
3
Understand the concept of essential priority.
Essential origination is defined by being preceded by its cause in essence (sabq dhātī), not necessarily in time. Think of a caused eternal being: it does not begin in time but is still ontologically dependent.
This counters the misconception that every originated thing must have a temporal start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد السادس : أبحاث الحدوث
الحادث: إما زماني أو لا، وهو المسمى بالحادث الذاتي وبإزائه القديم الذاتي، فيكون الحادث بالتفسير الثاني أعم منه بالتفسير الأول؛ إذ المعلول القديم بحسب الزمان إن ثبت كان حادثاً بهذا المعنى الثاني لأن كل معلول مسبوق بغيره الذي هو علته سبقاً ذاتياً، دون المعنى الأول.
The Third Observation - The Sixth Aim: Investigations of Origination. The originated: either temporal or not, and it is called essential origination, and opposite it is the eternal by essence. Thus the originated according to the second interpretation is more general than according to the first interpretation; for the eternal effect according to time, if proven, would be originated in this second sense because every effect is preceded by another [thing] which is its cause, by essential priority, not in the first sense.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage defines 'essential origination' (al-huduth al-dhati) as a type of origination that is not temporal. The author explains that the term 'originated' (al-hadith) can be interpreted in two ways: first, as temporally originated (al-hadith al-zamani), and second, as essentially originated (al-hadith al-dhati). The latter is broader because it includes things that are eternal in time but still caused. For example, if an effect is eternal in time (like the world according to some philosophers), it is not originated in the first sense (temporal) but is originated in the second sense because it is preceded by its cause in essence (i.e., it depends on the cause). The key point is that essential priority does not require temporal priority.

Key Terms
الحدوث الذاتي (al-huduth al-dhati)
القديم الذاتي (al-qadim al-dhati)
السبق الذاتي (al-sabq al-dhati)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often think 'originated' always means 'began in time'.
Correction: The passage clarifies that there is a type of origination—essential origination—that does not require a temporal beginning. Something can be eternal in time yet still be originated because it depends on a cause.

Checkpoint
Why is the second interpretation (essential origination) more general than the first (temporal origination)?
A) It includes things that are not caused.
B) It includes temporally eternal effects that are caused.
C) It excludes any eternal things.
D) It is the same as temporal origination.

Key Takeaway
Essential origination (al-ḥudūth al-dhātī) refers to causal dependence of an effect on its cause, regardless of temporal beginnings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you live with that constant awareness, or do you subconsciously act as if you and what you own are self-sufficient? What one practical change can you make today to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
When you succeed or achieve something, do you attribute it to your own ability or to the One who causes your existence at every instant? How does the concept of essential origination challenge your sense of self-reliance?
?
If even a temporally eternal thing would still be caused, then your own existence is perpetually contingent. Does that thought make you feel vulnerable or liberated? How will you respond the next time you feel independent of Allah?

The Two Senses of Huduth: Temporal and Essential
A nukta clarifying the two senses of huduth: temporal and essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of huduth (temporal and essential) and how they are distinguished by what precedes the originated thing.

Core Idea
Huduth has two senses depending on whether the priority is of non-existence or of the cause.

This lesson presents a precise theological distinction that clarifies a key concept in kalam. The nukta explains that huduth (origination) is defined by being preceded by something, and that something determines whether huduth is temporal or essential. Understanding this distinction is crucial for discussions about the nature of possible beings and the uniqueness of God's pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the prerequisite
Note that the distinction between existence and quiddity in possibles is necessary for the concept of non-necessitation of existence.
This sets the stage for the nukta.
2
Define huduth
Huduth is defined as the priority (mashuqiyya) of the existence of something, so it requires something prior.
The prior is either non-existence or another thing.
3
Identify the two types of priority
The preceding thing can be non-existence (incompatible with existence) or a cause (compatible).
4
Distinguish temporal and essential huduth
If the priority is of non-existence, huduth is temporal (zamani). If the priority is of a cause, huduth is essential (dhati).
Essential huduth applies to all possible beings.
5
Conclude the uniqueness of God
Essential pre-eternity (qidam dhati) is exclusive to the Necessary Existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد السادس : أبحاث الحدوث
اجتماعه يثبت ذلك بما ذكروه وعلى تقدير ثبوته إنما يصح هذا إذا قلنا : الوجود غير الماهية) في الممكنات، حتى يتصور هناك أن لا اقتضائها الوجود مقدم على اقتضائه؛ إذ لو كان الوجود عينها لم يتصور ذلك أصلاً . (نكتة) الحدوث لا يعقل إلا بسبق أمر عليه ) أي على الحادث لأن الحدوث عبارة عن مسبوقية وجود الشيء فلا يعقل إلا بأمر سابق عليه ( فهو ) أي ذلك السابق (إما عدمه ) الذي يمتنع مع اللاحق، أو أمر آخر يمكن اجتماعه معه . ( وإنما اختلف تفسيره نظرا إليه ) أي إلى ذلك الأمر، فإذا اعتبر تقدم العدم كان الحدوث زمانياً، لامتناع اجتماع المتقدم والمتأخر. وإذا اعتبر تقدم غير العدم وهو العلة كان الحدوث ذاتياً شاملاً للممكنات بأسرها اتفاقاً؛ لأن كل ممكن مسبوق بعلته سبقاً يجامع فيه السابق اللاحق فيكون القدم الذاتي مختصاً بالواجب تعالى
The Third Observatory - The Sixth Objective: Investigations of Origination. Its coming together is established by what they mentioned, and assuming its establishment, this is only correct if we say that existence is other than quiddity in possibles, so that it can be conceived that their non-necessitation of existence is prior to their necessitation of it; for if existence were identical to it, that would not be conceivable at all. (A precise point) Origination (al-huduth) is not conceivable except by the priority of something over it, i.e., over the originated thing, because huduth is the priority of the existence of something, so it is not conceivable except by something prior to it. So that something prior is either its non-existence, which is incompatible with the subsequent, or another thing that can coexist with it. And the difference in its interpretation is only with respect to that something. So if the priority of non-existence is considered, huduth is temporal, because of the impossibility of coexistence of the prior and the subsequent. And if the priority of other than non-existence, i.e., the cause, is considered, huduth is essential, covering all possibles unanimously, because every possible is preceded by its cause in a precedence in which the prior and the subsequent coexist. Thus essential pre-eternity is specific to the Necessary, exalted.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents a 'nukta' (a precise point) that clarifies the definition of huduth (origination) and the source of the disagreement over its types. It begins by establishing a prerequisite: that the distinction between existence and quiddity (essence) in possibles is necessary for the concept of non-necessitation of existence. Then it states that huduth is inconceivable except through something preceding the originated thing, because huduth is defined as the priority (mashuqiyya) of the existence of something. The preceding thing is either (a) the non-existence of the originated, which cannot coexist with it, or (b) another thing (such as the cause) which can coexist with it. The difference in interpreting huduth hinges on which of these two is considered. If the priority of non-existence is taken, then huduth is temporal (zamani), because the prior (non-existence) and the posterior (existence) cannot coexist. If the priority of something other than non-existence, namely the cause, is taken, then huduth is essential (dhati), and it applies to all possibles unanimously, since every possible is preceded by its cause in a way that the cause and the effect can coexist. Consequently, essential pre-eternity (qidam dhati) is unique to the Necessary Existence (God).

Key Terms
حدوث
زماني
ذاتي
مسبوقية
علة
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Essential huduth means being preceded by non-existence.
Correction: Essential huduth means being preceded by a cause, not non-existence. Non-existence priority gives temporal huduth.

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of temporal huduth (al-huduth al-zamani) according to the nukta?
A) It is being preceded by a cause that coexists with it.
B) It is being preceded by non-existence that is incompatible with the existence.
C) It applies to all possibles.
D) It is the same as essential huduth.

Key Takeaway
Huduth is defined by priority of something; if that something is non-existence, huduth is temporal; if it is a cause, huduth is essential, and essential pre-eternity belongs only to God.

?
When you consider your own origins, do you feel a temporal gap (a past when you did not exist) or a causal gap (your constant need for Allah’s preservation)? How does each perspective shape your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
The passage declares that essential pre-eternity belongs only to Allah. Does your heart attach itself to anything besides Allah as though it were pre-eternal? What created thing have you treated as if it has always been there for you? How can you reorient that attachment?

Possibility is Not an External Entity: Responding to Imam al-Razi's Objection
Commentary refuting an objection by distinguishing two types of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility (imkan) is not an external entity and how distinguishing between absolute essential possibility and temporal possibility resolves the regress objection.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental relation, not an external thing; conflating its types leads to false regress.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a critical objection by Imam al-Razi. He argued that if possibility existed, it would be either necessary or possible, leading to an infinite regress. The author clarifies that possibility is not an external entity but a mental consideration, and distinguishes two types to refute the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the objection and response structure.
The Arabic contains a typo: 'الإمان' should be 'الإمكان'.
2
Identify al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argued that if possibility existed, it would be either necessary or possible. Both are impossible: necessary because it is an attribute of something else, possible because it would lead to regress.
3
Understand the distinction
The author distinguishes between absolute essential possibility (salb darurat al-tarafayn) and temporal possibility (imkan al-huduth). The mental relation pertains only to the former.
Absolute essential possibility means negation of necessity from both existence and non-existence.
4
See how the regress stops
Since the mental relation concerns only essential possibility, the temporal possibility under discussion does not regenerate. Thus al-Razi's objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس هو بموجود في الخارج؛ إذ ليس لنا في الخارج شيء هو إمكان، بل هو إمكان وجود في الخارج. ولتعلقه بذلك الشيء يدل على وجود ذلك الشيء في الخارج، وهو موضوعه وفيه بحث، لأن تعلقه بذلك الشيء الذي هو موضوعه تعلق ذهني خارجي، فلا يدل على وجوده في الخارج. والمقصود دفع ما أورده الإمام من أن الإمان كان موجودا لكان واجبا أو ممكنا، والأول محال لكونه وصفا لغيره، والثاني محال لأنه يلزم أن يكون للإمكان إمكان. قوله: (وفيه بحث لأن تعلقه إلخ) قد ظهر لك اندفاعه بما حررناه لك؛ لأن التعلق الذهني إنما هو للإمكان الذاتي المطلق، أعني سلب ضرورة الطرفين دون إمكان الحدوث أعني إمكان وجوده بعد العدم.
It does not exist externally; for we have nothing in the external world that is possibility, rather it is the possibility of existence in the external world. And its relation to that thing indicates the existence of that thing in the external world, which is its subject, and there is a discussion therein, because its relation to that thing which is its subject is a mental-external relation, so it does not indicate its existence in the external world. The purpose is to refute what al-Imam raised: that if possibility existed, it would be either necessary (wajib) or possible (mumkin). The first is impossible because it is an attribute of something else; the second is impossible because it would entail that possibility has a possibility. His statement: (And there is a discussion there because its relation etc.) has been shown to you to be repelled by what we have clarified for you; for the mental relation pertains only to absolute essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati al-mutlaq), namely the negation of necessity of both sides, not temporal possibility (imkan al-huduth), namely the possibility of its existence after non-existence.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by Imam al-Razi. The author begins by clarifying the nature of possibility (imkan): it does not exist as an external entity; rather, it is a mental consideration relating to an external existent. An objection is then stated: if possibility existed, it would itself be either necessary or possible, leading to regress. The author responds by distinguishing between two types of possibility: absolute essential possibility (salb darurat al-tarafayn) and temporal possibility (imkan al-huduth). The mental relation pertains only to the former, thus the regress stops. The 'discussion' (bahth) referred to is answered by noting that the mental relation concerns essential possibility, not the temporal possibility that is under discussion.

إمكان
الإمكان الذاتي المطلق
إمكان الحدوث
سلب ضرورة الطرفين
تعلق ذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is an external existent that would itself be either necessary or possible, leading to an infinite regress.
Correction: Possibility is not an external entity; it is a mental relation. The regress is avoided by distinguishing absolute essential possibility from temporal possibility.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Possibility does not exist as an external entity; the mental relation of possibility pertains only to absolute essential possibility, not temporal possibility.

Unity is Coextensive with Existence
Defines unity and explains that even multiplicity has a kind of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity is said to be coextensive with existence and how multiplicity itself possesses a kind of unity without contradiction.

Core Idea
Unity and existence are inseparable — even multiplicity has its own unity.

This lesson explores the fundamental concept that unity (waḥda) is not just an attribute of singular things, but is coextensive with existence itself. The author establishes that every existent, even multiplicity (kathra), has some form of unity. We'll examine how this is possible and what it means for the relationship between unity and multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and the use of parentheses for commentary.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that unity is coextensive with existence (الوحدة تساوق الوجود). Every existent has some unity, and everything with unity exists. Note the phrase 'in summary' (في الجملة) which includes mental existence.
3
Grasp the example of multiplicity
The author uses the example of 'ten' being one ten among tens. Even multiplicity (kathra) has a unitive quiddity (māhiyya waḥdāniyya). Understand that multiplicity as a whole has unity, not each individual part.
This resolves the apparent contradiction: unity attaches to the multiplicity (al-kathra), not to the many (al-kathīr).
4
Understand the resolution of opposition
The passage explains that unity and multiplicity do not attach to the same subject; unity attaches to multiplicity considered as a whole, so they are not truly opposites in one subject. This is consistent with logical opposition rules.
The commentary clarifies that describing the many with unity involves mediately observing the multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع: في الوحدة والكثرة فإنهما من الأمور العامة العارضة للموجودات الخارجية والذهنية . وفيه مقاصد : المقصد الأول : الوحدة تساوق الوجود . الوحدة تساوق الوجود أي تساويه، فكل ماله وحدة فهو موجود في الجملة، وكل موجود له وحدة ما حتى الكثير الذي هو أبعد الأشياء عن الوحدة، إذ كل كثير يحصل له ماهية وحدانية ما هو عين الاتصاف بالوحدة، فإن العشرة المخصوصة مثلاً عشرة واحدة من العشرات وهو أي اتصاف الكثير بالوحدة لا يمنع تقابلهما أي تقابل الوحدة والكثرة، فإنهما لم يعرضا لشيء واحد . نعم عرض الوحدة للكثرة لا للكثير الذي عرض له الكثرة، ولا استحالة في عروض . قوله : ( فإنهما من الأمور إلخ) تعليل لإيرادهما في مرصد على حدة مع كونهما من لواحق الماهية، ولذا ذكرهما صاحب التجريد في فصل الماهية وليس المقصود بيان كونهما من الأمور العامة، فإنه مذكور في تعريف الأمور العامة بما لا مزيد عليه . قوله : ( والذهنية) ذكره استطراداً كيلا يتوهم من الاكتفاء بالخارجية الاختصاص بها، وإلا فلا دخل له في كونهما من الأمور العامة . قوله : ( في الجملة) أي خارجاً أو ذهناً . قوله : ( وحدة ما ) أي حقيقية أو اعتبارية . قوله : أي اتصاف الكثير بالوحدة أي بتوسط ملاحظة الكثرة معه، كما يدل عليه قوله فإن العشرة المخصوصة إلخ، فلا يرد أن اتصاف الكثير بالوحدة اجتماع المتقابلين في موضوع يمنع تقابلهما . ثم إنه يمتنع اجتماع المتقابلين بالذات في محل واحد واحد، فكيف لا كما قوله : ( فهو موجود في الجملة أي إما في الخارج أو في الذهن، فلا يرد أن الكلي الطبيعي له وحدة وليس بموجود .
The Fourth Observatory: On Unity and Multiplicity. For indeed they are among the universal matters (al-umūr al-ʿāmmah) that attach to external and mental existents. It contains purposes. The First Purpose: Unity is coextensive with existence. Unity is coextensive with existence, i.e., equal to it. So everything that has unity is existent in summary (fī l-jumla), and every existent has some unity (waḥda mā), even the many (al-kathīr) which is the furthest thing from unity, for every many (kathīr) has a unitive quiddity (māhiyya waḥdāniyya) which is the very description with unity. For example, the specific ten is one ten among tens. And it—i.e., the description of the many with unity—does not prevent their opposition (taqābaluhumā), i.e., the opposition of unity and multiplicity, for they do not attach to one single thing. Rather, unity attaches to multiplicity (al-kathra), not to the many (al-kathīr) which multiplicity attaches to, and there is no impossibility in attachment. His saying (For indeed they are among the universal matters etc.) is a reason for adducing them in a separate observatory despite them being among the concomitants of quiddity, and therefore the author of al-Tajrīd mentioned them in the chapter on quiddity. And the intent is not to state that they are among the universal matters, for that is mentioned in the definition of universal matters with no more than that. His saying (and mental) he mentioned digressively so that one might not imagine from restricting to external that they are specific to it; otherwise it has no bearing on them being universal matters. His saying (in summary) means either externally or mentally. His saying (some unity) means either real (ḥaqīqiyya) or relative (iʿtibāriyya). His saying (i.e., the description of the many with unity means through mediating the observation of multiplicity along with it, as is indicated by his saying 'for the specific ten' etc., so it is not objected that the description of the many with unity is a conjunction of two opposites in one subject that prevents their opposition. Then it is impossible for two essentially opposite things to exist together in one single locus; how could it not be? As his saying (So it is existent in summary means either externally or mentally, so it is not objected that the natural universal has unity but is not existent.)
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of unity as a universal matter. It first states that unity is coextensive with existence: everything with unity exists, and every existent has some unity, even multiplicity. The example of 'ten' shows that multiplicity itself has a unitive aspect (being one ten). The author then addresses a potential objection: unity and multiplicity are opposites, yet multiplicity is described as having unity. He resolves this by saying that unity attaches to the multiplicity, not to the many itself. The commentary then explains the reason for treating these topics separately, clarifies that 'in summary' includes mental existence, and that 'some unity' can be real or relative. It further explains that describing the many with unity is not a contradiction because it involves observing the multiplicity as a whole. Finally, it notes that the natural universal (al-kullī al-ṭabīʿī) has unity but is not existent externally, only mentally, so the condition 'existent in summary' covers that.

كثرة
وجود
أمور عامة
ماهية
اعتباري
كلي طبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that unity cannot apply to multiplicity because they are opposites.
Correction: The passage clarifies that unity attaches to the multiplicity (kathra) as a whole, not to the individual many (kathīr), so no contradiction arises.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that describing the many with unity does not imply opposition between unity and multiplicity?
A) Because unity and multiplicity are not truly opposites.
B) Because unity attaches to the multiplicity, not to the many itself.
C) Because the many has no unity at all.
D) Because opposition only occurs in external existents.

Key Takeaway
Unity is inseparable from existence; even multiplicity has a kind of unity when considered as a whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist, so you have unity. But when you look at the many roles you play—parent, worker, friend—do you experience yourself as a single, coherent being, or do you feel fragmented? What would change in your life if you truly embraced the unitive quiddity that the passage says every existent possesses? Choose one concrete action today that aligns your scattered actions into a unified expression of your faith.
?
The passage teaches that even multiplicity (like the number ten) has a unitive quiddity. Think of the most divided or contradictory area of your life. Can you see how it might still have a hidden unity, a single thread that ties it together? If you cannot, what does that reveal about your understanding of yourself? Will you ask Allah to show you the unity He has placed even in your chaos?
?
Unity and existence are inseparable. If you are a believer, your existence is a gift from Allah. Yet do you live as if your existence is truly unified under His oneness (tawḥīd)? In what specific way have you allowed the 'many' in your life to obscure the unity that should define you? Make a firm intention (niyya) tonight to prioritize the one thing that gives your life coherence: your relationship with Allah.

Unity Accompanies Existence
Sanusi defines unity as coextensive with existence and addresses its application to the many.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine that unity and existence are coextensive, and how unity can be attributed to the many without contradiction.

Core Idea
Unity and existence are two sides of the same coin.

This passage from the fourth observatory establishes the foundational relationship between unity and existence. The author explains that wherever there is unity, there is existence, and vice versa. He then addresses a potential puzzle: how can the many, which seem opposed to unity, still be described as having unity? The solution lies in understanding that unity attaches to plurality itself, not to the many as the subject of plurality.

Guided Path
1
Read the core thesis
Read the statement 'الوحدة تساوق الوجود' and its immediate explanation. Notice that the author asserts equality between unity and existence.
The commentary clarifies 'في الجملة' means externally or mentally.
2
Understand the attribution of unity to the many
The passage explains that every existent has some unity, even the many. Focus on the example of 'ten' being one of the tens.
This shows that unity can apply to plurality in a mediated way.
3
Resolve the apparent contradiction
Study the claim that unity and plurality do not oppose each other when they occur to different subjects. Unity occurs to plurality, not to the many itself.
This prevents the conjunction of opposites in one subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع: في الوحدة والكثرة فإنهما من الأمور العامة العارضة للموجودات الخارجية والذهنية . ( وفيه مقاصد ) : [ المقصد الأول : الوحدة تساوق الوجود ] الاتصاف ( الوحدة تساوق الوجود أي تساويه، فكل ماله وحدة فهو موجود في الجملة، ( وكل موجود له وحدة ما (حتى الكثير) الذي هو أبعد الأشياء عن ) بالوحدة، إذ كل كثير يحصل له ماهية وحدانية ما هو عين الاتصاف بالوحدة، (فإن العشرة المخصوصة مثلاً عشرة ) واحدة من العشرات وهو) أي اتصاف الكثير بالوحدة ( لا يمنع تقابلهما ) أي تقابل الوحدة الكثرة، فإنهما لم يعرضا لشيء واحد . نعم عرض الوحدة للكثرة لا للكثير الذي عرض له الكثرة، ولا استحالة في عروض
قوله : ( فإنهما من الأمور إلخ) تعليل لإيرادهما في مرصد على حدة مع كونهما من لواحق الماهية، ولذا ذكرهما صاحب التجريد في فصل الماهية وليس المقصود بيان كونهما من الأمور العامة، فإنه مذكور في تعريف الأمور العامة بما لا مزيد عليه .
قوله : ( والذهنية) ذكره استطراداً كيلا يتوهم من الاكتفاء بالخارجية الاختصاص بها، وإلا فلا دخل له في كونهما من الأمور العامة. قوله : ( في الجملة) أي خارجاً أو ذهناً.
قوله : ( وحدة ما ) أي حقيقية أو اعتبارية .
قوله : أي اتصاف الكثير بالوحدة أي بتوسط ملاحظة الكثرة معه، كما يدل عليه قوله فإن العشرة المخصوصة إلخ، فلا يرد أن اتصاف الكثير بالوحدة اجتماع المتقابلين في موضوع يمنع تقابلهما. ثم إنه يمتنع اجتماع المتقابلين بالذات في محل واحد

قوله: (فإنهما لم يعرضا لشيء واحد إلخ) فإن قلت : لهذا الكلام محل غير ما ذكره الشارح، لا يحتاج فيه إلى هذه التطويلات المذكورة، ولا يرد الاعتراض الآتي ابتداء، وهو أن اللام لام الأجل ،والسبب لاصلة العروض أي لم يعرضا لأجل شيء ،واحد بل عروض الوحدة لأجل الكثرة . قلت : يأباه قول المصنف لا الكثير، فإن المفهوم منه على ذلك المحمل أن الكثرة تعرض لأجل الكثير، وإلا للغا هذا النفي، ولا معنى لأن يقال : عروض الكثرة لأجل الكثير. اللهم إلا أن يقال : معناه يعرض الكثرة للكثير لأجل نفسه أي لذاته.
The fourth observatory: on unity and plurality. For they are among the universal matters that occur to external and mental existents. (And in it are [several] purposes): [The first purpose: unity accompanies existence.] Attribution (Unity accompanies existence, i.e., is equal to it. So everything that has unity is existent in a general sense, and every existent has some unity (even the many) which is the furthest thing from unity, for every many has a certain unitary quiddity which is the very attribution with unity. (For example, the specific ten is one of the tens and it) i.e., the attribution of the many with unity (does not prevent their contrast) i.e., the contrast of unity and plurality, for they do not occur to one thing. Rather, unity occurs to plurality, not to the many to which plurality occurs, and there is no impossibility in the occurrence of one of the two opposites to the other. His statement: (For they are among the matters etc.) is a rationale for introducing them in a separate observatory despite their being among the accidents of quiddity, hence the author of al-Tajrid mentioned them in the chapter on quiddity, and the intent is not to show that they are among the universal matters, for that is mentioned in the definition of universal matters in the most complete manner. His statement: (And mental) he mentioned it parenthetically so that one would not imagine from restricting to external that they are specific to it, otherwise it has no bearing on their being among the universal matters. His statement: (In a general sense) i.e., externally or mentally. His statement: (Some unity) i.e., real or relational. His statement: i.e., the attribution of the many with unity, i.e., by means of observing plurality with it, as indicated by his statement: for the specific ten etc., so it is not objected that the attribution of the many with unity is the conjunction of two opposites in one subject which would prevent their contrast. Then, the conjunction of opposites in essence in one place is impossible. His statement: (So he is existent in a general sense) i.e., either externally or mentally, so it is not objected that the natural universal has unity but is not existent. His statement: (For they do not occur to one thing etc.) For you say: This statement has a place other than what the commentator mentioned; it does not need these lengthy discussions, nor does the following objection arise initially, namely that the lam is the lam of purpose, and the cause is for the occurrence, i.e., they do not occur for the sake of one thing, rather the occurrence of unity is for the sake of plurality. I say: The author's statement 'not the many' precludes it, for the understanding from that interpretation is that plurality occurs for the sake of the many, otherwise this negation would be pointless, and there is no meaning to saying: the occurrence of plurality for the sake of the many. Unless it is said: its meaning is that plurality occurs to the many for the sake of itself, i.e., its essence.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a definition and initial explanation of the first purpose of the fourth observatory. The author begins by stating that unity (al-wahda) and plurality (al-kathra) are universal matters that apply to both external and mental existents. He then lays down the fundamental thesis: 'Unity accompanies existence' meaning they are coextensive. The commentary explains that 'in a general sense' means either externally or mentally, answering a potential objection about the natural universal. The phrase 'some unity' is clarified as real or relational. A key point is that the many can be described as having unity through a mediated observation of plurality, as in the example of the specific ten. The commentator addresses a potential objection about the conjunction of opposites, explaining that unity and plurality do not occur to the same subject in the same respect; rather, unity occurs to plurality itself, not to the many which is the subject of plurality. The passage then presents an objection regarding the interpretation of the preposition 'lam' and answers it by showing that the author's wording 'not the many' prevents the alternative interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that unity and plurality cannot both apply to the same thing because they are opposites.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they apply to different aspects: unity applies to plurality as a whole, not to the individual many that are subjects of plurality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'unity accompanies existence' mean?
A) Unity and plurality are always in conflict.
B) Everything that has unity exists, and everything that exists has some unity.
C) Unity exists only in the mind, not in external reality.
D) The many can never have unity.

Key Takeaway
Unity and existence are coextensive, and unity can be attributed to the many without contradiction because it applies to plurality as a whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which of these do you treat as fragmented and disconnected from Allah's oneness? What would it mean for you to see them all as unified under His tawhid? Commit to one specific shift in perspective today.
?
Your life is made of many moments, duties, and desires—like the many (الكثير) that nevertheless has a unitary quiddity. Are you living your life as a scattered collection or as a unified whole directed toward a single purpose? What one distraction or habit breaks that unity? Identify it and resolve to align it with your ultimate goal.
?
The principle that opposites can apply to the same thing in different respects resolves apparent contradictions. Think of a tension you face between worldly responsibilities and spiritual devotion. How can this principle help you see them not as contradictory but as two aspects of a unified life? Write down a practical step to integrate them instead of compartmentalizing.

The Definition of Multiplicity and Its Relation to Unity
Commentator explains how multiplicity is defined through unity and the nature of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how multiplicity is defined through unity and why this is considered a real definition in 'ilm al-kalam.

Core Idea
Multiplicity is known through unity, and this definition is a valid real definition by the part.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a subtle point: how do we define multiplicity when it seems to be the opposite of unity? He explains that the definition works by pointing to an intuitive knowledge through an overall essential concept. We'll see how this fits into the larger kalam project of defining terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
The text is dense; don't worry about understanding every detail at first.
2
Identify the universal vs particular distinction
Notice how the commentator explains unity and multiplicity both as universals and as particulars. This is key to his argument.
Universal unity exists within particular unity; universal multiplicity arises after observing multiple universal matters.
3
Understand the order of perception
For particulars, the commentator states that particular multiplicity of imaginary forms is perceived before their particular unity. Reflect on why that might be.
This is due to not attending to unity during the act of indication.
4
Grasp the nature of the definition
The definition is described as 'indication' (tanbīh) of an intuitive knowledge via an overall essential concept. This means it points to something already known.
The term 'al-kunh al-ijmālī' is crucial.
5
Learn why it is a real definition
The commentator calls it 'real' (ḥaqīqī) because it defines by the part, even if that part is not predicated of the whole.
This addresses a potential objection about circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فالوحدة إلخ) أي من حيث إنها مدركة وكذا في قوله : والكثرة عارضة . قوله : ( سواء أخذ كليين) أما الكليان فإن الوحدة الكلية حاصلة في ضمن الوحدة الجزئية العارضة للأمر الكلي المشترك، والكثرة الكلية إنما تحصل بعد ملاحظة الأمور الكلية الحاصلة ذات النفس من حيث إنها متكثرة، وأما الجزئيتان فلان الكثرة الجزئية العارضة للصور الخيالية حاصلة قبل حصول الوحدة الجزئية الخيالية العارضة لكل واحدة منها؛ لعدم الالتفات إليه من حيث وحدته حال التنبيه المذكور.
قوله : ( فيجوز التنبيه إشارة إلى أن التعريف المذكور يكون من قبيل التنبيه على معرفة كل منهما الحاصلة بالبداهة بطريق الكنه الإجمالي .
قوله : (تعريف حقيقي) لأنه تعريف بالجزء، وإن كان غير محمول.
His statement: '(Thus the unity, etc.)' means: from the aspect that it is perceived, and likewise in his statement: 'and multiplicity is accidental.' His statement: '(whether taken as universals)' — as for the two universals, universal unity exists within particular unity that is accidental to the common universal matter, and universal multiplicity is only obtained after observing the universal matters that occur in the soul itself from the aspect that it is multiple. As for the two particulars, the particular multiplicity accidental to imaginary forms is obtained before the particular imaginary unity accidental to each one of them, due to the lack of attention to it from the aspect of its unity during the mentioned indication. His statement: '(Thus indication is possible)' is an indication that the mentioned definition is of the type of indication to the knowledge of each of them that is obtained intuitively by way of an overall essential concept. His statement: '(A real definition)' because it is a definition by the part, even if it is not predicated.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the definition of multiplicity and its relation to unity. The commentator begins by clarifying the phrase 'Thus the unity, etc.' from the main text, explaining that it refers to unity insofar as it is perceived, and similarly for multiplicity as an accident. He then addresses the phrase 'whether taken as universals or particulars.' He explains that when taken as universals, universal unity is subsumed under the particular unity that is accidental to a common universal matter, while universal multiplicity is obtained only after observing the universal matters present in the soul itself as multiple. For the two particulars, the particular multiplicity accidental to imaginary forms occurs before the particular imaginary unity accidental to each form, because during the act of indicating one does not attend to the form's unity. Next, the commentator remarks on the phrase 'thus indication is possible,' noting that the definition given is a kind of indication to an intuitive knowledge of each (unity and multiplicity) via an overall essential concept (al-kunh al-ijmālī). Finally, he explains why the definition is called 'real' (ḥaqīqī): it is a definition by the part (al-juzʾ), even though that part is not predicated of the whole. This entire passage shows how the author carefully distinguishes the epistemic priority of unity over multiplicity.

Key Terms

الكثرة
تعريف حقيقي
الكنه الإجمالي
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining multiplicity by unity is circular, but the commentator explains it is a real definition by the part.
Correction: The definition is not circular because it identifies a part (unity) that is essential to the concept of multiplicity, and this part is not predicated of the whole, fitting the criteria for a real definition.

Checkpoint
Why is the definition of multiplicity by unity considered a real (ḥaqīqī) definition according to the commentator?
A) Because it is a definition by the genus and differentia.
B) Because it is a definition by the part, even if that part is not predicated.
C) Because it is a nominal definition.
D) Because it includes both unity and multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity is defined by unity through an intuitive overall essential concept, and this is a real definition because it identifies a part even if not predicated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In what area of your life are you most prone to seeing multiplicity without acknowledging the underlying unity? What will you do today to correct that?
?
The commentator says the definition is by 'indication' pointing to an intuitive knowledge. When have you experienced an intuitive recognition of Allah's unity that was not based on detailed reasoning? How can you cultivate that intuitive awareness in your daily life?
?
If the definition is by a part that is not predicated, then understanding reality often requires seeing the whole through its components without reducing it. In your own spiritual journey, what 'part' of your faith do you sometimes focus on instead of the whole picture of submission to Allah? How will you re-center?

Classification of the One of Person
Defines divisions of al-wahid bi al-shakhs into point, separate, and continuous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divisions of al-wahid bi al-shakhs into point, separate, and continuous, and how they relate to the concept of non-division.

Core Idea
The one of person is defined by indivisibility, but if it has a further concept, it is either a point, a separate entity, or a continuous whole.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary breaks down the concept of 'the one of person' (al-wahid bi al-shakhs) into its key divisions. The classification clarifies how the notion of non-division applies to different kinds of entities—those that have a position (points), those that do not (separate, like God), and those that are divisible into similar parts (continuous). Understanding this taxonomy is foundational for later discussions about the attributes of God and the nature of created beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its English translation to get the overall classification.
Notice that the passage distinguishes between having a concept beyond non-division or not.
2
Identify the two main branches
The first division: if the one of person has a concept other than non-division, it is either a point (has position) or a separate (no position). If it has no other concept, it remains as the simple notion of non-division.
The commentary clarifies that 'personal unity' is an individual instance of that concept.
3
Understand the second division
If the one of person accepts division, it divides into similar quantitative parts, which is the one by continuity (al-wahid bi al-ittisal).
This adds a third type under a different condition.
4
Study the commentary explanations
The commentary unpacks each phrase: 'personal unity' enters into the divided category, 'other than that' refers to non-division as an accident, 'point' is defined relative to atomism, and 'separate' includes both necessary and possible existents.
These clarifications prevent misunderstandings about the scope of each division.
5
Reflect on the overall taxonomy
Consider how this classification sets the stage for discussions about the oneness of God and the composition of bodies.
The one of person is a broad category; not all its members are indivisible in every sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينقسم أي سوى مفهوم عدم الانقسام (فالوحدة) الشخصية ( وإن كان ) له مفهوم سوى ذلك فإما ذو وضع أي قابل للإشارة الحسية (وهو النقطة) المشخصة ( أولا ) يكون ذو وضع، وهو المفارق المشخص . ( وإن قبل ( الواحد بالشخص ( القسمة فإما ) أن ينقسم إلى أجزاء ) مقدارية ( متشابهة) في الحقيقة (وهو الواحد بالاتصال) 
قوله: (فالوحدة الشخصية) أي فرد من أفرادها وذلك لتكون داخلة في المقسم أعني الواحد بالشخص، وكذا الحال في البواقي .
قوله : (سوى ذلك أي عدم الانقسام فيكون عارضاً لماهية. قوله : ( وهو النقطة) عند نفاة الجزء، وإن أريد أعم من الجوهرية والعرضية يصح على رأي مثبتيه أيضاً .
قوله : ( وهو المفارق أعم من أن يكون واجباً أو ممكناً.
It is divided: i.e., other than the concept of non-division (so it is) personal unity (and if) it has a concept other than that, then either it has a position, i.e., is subject to sensory indication (and it is the point) that is individuated, or it does not have a position, and it is the separate individuated thing. And if the one of person accepts division, then either it divides into quantitative parts that are similar in reality (and it is the one by continuity).
His saying: (personal unity): i.e., an individual of its individuals, so that it enters into the divided, i.e., the one of person, and similarly for the rest.
His saying: (other than that): i.e., non-division, so it is an accident of the essence.
His saying: (and it is the point): according to the deniers of the part, and if what is meant is more general than substance and accident, it is valid also according to the opinion of the affirmers.
His saying: (and it is the separate): more general than being necessary or possible.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the divisions of the one of person (al-wahid bi al-shakhs). The main text states that the one of person is that which has no concept other than non-division. If it has a concept other than that, then it is either something with a position (i.e., perceptible by the senses), which is the point (al-nuqta) that individuates, or without a position, which is the separate individuated thing (al-mufariq al-mushakhkhas). Then it adds that if the one of person accepts division, it divides into quantitative parts that are similar in reality, and that is the one by continuity (al-wahid bi al-ittisal). The commentary explains each phrase: 'personal unity' means an individual of its individuals, so that it falls under the divided category; 'other than that' means non-division, so it is an accident of the essence; 'the point' is defined according to the deniers of the part (i.e., those who deny atomism), but if taken more broadly it also works for affirmers; 'the separate' includes both necessary (i.e., God) and possible beings.

Key Terms
الواحد بالشخص
الوحدة الشخصية
النقطة
المفارق
الواحد بالاتصال
عدم الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The one of person must always have a concept beyond non-division.
Correction: Sometimes the one of person is simply the notion of non-division with no additional concept, as when it is just the accident of non-division.

Checkpoint
How many categories of al-wahid bi al-shakhs (one of person) with a concept beyond non-division are explicitly mentioned in the passage?
A) Two: point and separate
B) Three: point, separate, and continuous
C) Four: point, separate, continuous, and pure non-division
D) One: only the point

Key Takeaway
The one of person is defined by indivisibility, but its instances can be points, separate entities, or continuous wholes depending on additional attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Ahad', do you truly affirm that He is 'one of person' with no additional concept beyond indivisibility? How does your daily worship reflect that belief, or are there times you mentally assign parts to Him?
?
The passage distinguishes between perceptible points and separate entities. Since God is 'separate' without position, do you ever catch yourself imagining Him as being somewhere or having a location? What can you do to purify your conception of His oneness?
