Al-Ghayran: Definition and Its Application to God and the World
Defines al-ghayran and resolves an objection about God and the world via conceptual separability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ghayran (two distinct things) and how it is used to justify the need for a proof of God's existence, as well as the key distinction between conceptual and existential separation.

Core Idea
Two things are distinct if they are conceptually separable, not necessarily in reality.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces a crucial term in Islamic theology: al-ghayran, meaning two distinct entities. The discussion begins by defining this term and then applies it to the relationship between the world and God. A major objection is raised: since the world cannot exist without God, how can they be considered distinct? The answer lies in a subtle but essential distinction between conceptual separability and real separability. By the end, you will appreciate how theological reasoning depends on precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Definition
Read the Arabic definition of al-ghayran and its translation. Note the condition: it is permissible to know each while ignoring the other.
This definition is about knowledge, not existence.
2
See the Application to World and God
The author applies the definition to show that one can conceive of the world without God, hence a proof is needed. Ask yourself: why does this make a proof necessary?
3
Understand the Objection and Answer
The objection says the world cannot be separated from God in existence. The answer distinguishes between existential and conceptual separation. Notice how the answer relies on the definition.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Explore the Nuance with Qualifications
The author notes that if you add 'in non-existence' or 'in space' to the definition, the answer fails. This shows the importance of precision. Why does the answer fail? Think about what happens to the attribute and its subject.
This leads to a further problem about attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغيران هما اللذان يجوز العلم بكل منهما مع الجهل بالآخر، ( ولا يمتنع تعقل العالم ) والجزم بوجوده ( بدون ) تعقل ( الباري) والجزم بوجوده ، ( ولذلك يحتاج ) في وجود الباري بعد العلم بوجود العالم ) إلى الإثبات) بالبرهان وهذا الجواب . إنما يصح إذا عرف الغيران بأنهما موجودان يجوز الانفكاك بينهما من الجانبين ثم يعترض بالباري والعالم، فإنه لا يجوز انفكاك العالم عن الباري في الوجود فيجاب: بأن ليس المراد جواز الانفكاك الجانبين في الوجود، بل في التعقل ولا خفاء في جواز انفكاك من كل من العالم والصانع عن الآخر في التعقل، وأما إذا زيد في التعريف قيد في عدم أو حيز، فلا صحة لهذا الجواب، إذ لا يجوز أن يقال : يتعقل الباري معدوماً أو متحيزاً بدون أن يتعقل العالم كذلك، إلا إذا جوز كون التعقل أعم من أن يكون مطابقاً أو غيره. وحينئذ يلزم كون الصفة والموصوف متغايرين، إذ يجوز أن يتعقل وجود كل منهما بدون وجود الآخر، إما تعقلاً مطابقاً، أو غير مطابق.
The two distinct things (al-ghayrān) are those two [things] concerning which it is permissible to have knowledge of each one along with ignorance of the other. (And it is not impossible to conceive of the world) and to affirm its existence (without) conceiving (of the Creator) and affirming His existence. (And therefore it is necessary) after knowledge of the existence of the world (to prove the existence of the Creator) by demonstration. And this answer is only valid if the two distinct things are defined as two existents between which separation from both sides is permissible. Then an objection is raised concerning the Creator and the world: For it is not permissible for the world to be separated from the Creator in existence. So it is answered: The intention is not the permissibility of separation from both sides in existence, but rather in conception (ta‘aqqul). And there is no obscurity in the permissibility of separation of each of the world and the Maker from the other in conception. But if a qualification is added to the definition, [such as] "in non-existence" or "in space", then this answer is not valid, because it is not permissible to say: One conceives of the Creator as non-existent or as occupying space without conceiving of the world likewise, unless one allows conception to be more general, whether it is veridical or not. And then it follows that the attribute and the qualified would be two distinct things, for it is permissible to conceive of the existence of each one without the existence of the other, either by veridical conception or non-veridical.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines the term "al-ghayrān" (the two distinct things) and addresses a key objection regarding the relation between God and the world. The author begins by giving the definition: two things are distinct if it is possible to know each one while being ignorant of the other. He then applies this to the case of the world and the Creator: it is possible to conceive of the world and affirm its existence without conceiving of the Creator, which is why a proof is needed to establish the existence of the Creator after knowing the world. The answer to the objection that the world cannot be separated from God in existence is that the required separation is not in external existence but in conception (ta‘aqqul). Both the world and the Creator can be mentally conceived without the other. However, if one adds a qualifier to the definition, such as "in non-existence" or "in space", then this answer fails because one cannot conceive of God as non-existent or corporeal without also conceiving of the world as such, unless conception is taken broadly to include non-veridical conceptions. This leads to a further issue: if conceptual separation is allowed, then an attribute and its qualified would be considered distinct, which is problematic. The passage thus highlights the subtlety in defining distinctness.

Key Terms
الغيران
التعقل
الانفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distinctness requires existential separation; two things cannot be distinct if one depends on the other for existence.
Correction: Distinctness in Islamic theology is determined by conceptual separability: if you can know each without the other, they are distinct. Thus, the world and God are distinct even though the world depends on God for existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for two things being 'ghayrān' (distinct)?
A) They must be separable in external existence.
B) It is possible to know each one while being ignorant of the other.
C) They must be different in essence.
D) They must be independent in time.

Key Takeaway
Distinctness in Islamic theology is determined by conceptual separability, not existential independence, which allows us to speak of God and the world as distinct while acknowledging the world's dependence on God.

How can you train yourself to immediately link every thought of a problem to the reality of God's presence and power? Choose one situation today where you will consciously make that link.

The lesson says we can know the world without knowing God, and that is why we need proof for God. In your own life, have you settled for knowing the world while ignoring the Creator? What would it look like to actively seek God through the world, rather than letting your conception of the world remain Godless? Write down one step you will take this week to connect your daily experiences to divine proofs.

Is there any belief or habit you hold that treats the world as if it were God, or as if God were merely part of the world? The Majority's Objection to 'Neither Identical Nor Distinct'
Al-Ījī presents the majority's objection to the statement 'neither identical nor distinct' as positing an intermediary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the majority of theologians reject the statement that an attribute is neither identical to nor distinct from its qualified, as it posits an impossible intermediary between identity and distinctness.

Core Idea
The majority sees no middle ground: if not identical, then distinct.

In this passage, al-Ījī reports the strong rejection by the majority (al-jumhūr) of the controversial statement that an attribute is neither identical to nor distinct from its qualified. They argue that this claim introduces a third category between identity and distinctness, which is logically untenable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the controversy
Read the opening sentence where al-Ījī mentions that the majority find the statement 'neither identical nor distinct' improbable.
2
Grasp the logical reasoning
The passage explains that distinctness (al-ghayriyya) is simply the negation of identity (al-‘ayniyya). So there is no middle term: everything is either identical or distinct.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for theology: it preserves the law of excluded middle in metaphysical relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( واعلم أن قولهم) أي قول مشايخنا في الصفة مع الموصوف وفي الجزء مع الكل: (لا هو ولا غيره، مما استبعده (الجمهور) جدا ( فإنه إثبات للواسطة بين النفي والإثبات؛ إذ الغيرية تساوي نفي العينية، فكل ما ليس بعين فهو غير كما أن كل ما هو غير فليس بعين. ( ومنهم من اعتذر عن ذلك ) بأنه نزاع لفظي ( لا تعلق له بأمر معنوي؛ وذلك أن هؤلاء خصصوا لفظ الغير بأن اصطلحوا على أن الغيرين ما يجوز الانفكاك بينهما. وعلى هذا فالشيء بالقياس إلى آخر، قد لا يكون عيناً ولا غيراً، وإذا أجري لفظ الغير على معناه المشهور بلا تخصيص فكل شيء بالقياس إلى آخر، إما عين وإما غير.
(And know that their statement) – i.e., the statement of our scholars concerning the attribute with the qualified and concerning the part with the whole: (It is neither identical nor distinct) – is greatly deemed improbable by (the majority), (for it is a positing of an intermediary between negation and affirmation. Since distinctness (al-ghayriyya) is equivalent to negating identity (al-‘ayniyya), everything that is not identical is distinct, just as everything that is distinct is not identical. (And some of them excused this) by [saying] that it is a verbal dispute (that has no connection to a conceptual matter). That is because these [scholars] specified the word "al-ghayr" by stipulating that "the two distinct things (al-ghayrān)" are those between which separation is permissible. On this [definition], a thing in relation to another may be neither identical nor distinct. But if the word "al-ghayr" is used according to its well-known meaning without specification, then everything in relation to another is either identical or distinct.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses the controversial statement attributed to some early scholars concerning the relationship between an attribute and its qualified, and between a part and the whole: they said "it is neither identical nor distinct" (lā huwa wa lā ghayruhu). The majority of theologians (al-jumhūr) strongly reject this because it posits a third category between identity and distinctness. The author explains that distinctness (al-ghayriyya) is simply the negation of identity (al-‘ayniyya), so everything that is not identical is distinct, and vice versa. There is no middle ground. However, some scholars defend this statement by arguing that it is a verbal dispute (nizā‘ lafẓī) with no real conceptual import. They claim that those who made the statement had a technical definition of "al-ghayr": they defined "the two distinct things" as those that can be separated. According to this technical usage, a thing can be neither identical nor distinct. But if the term is used in its ordinary sense, then everything is either identical or distinct. Thus the disagreement is merely about the definition of the word, not about any real metaphysical fact.

Key Terms
الجمهور
لا هو ولا غيره
الغيرية
العينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the statement 'neither identical nor distinct' is a profound metaphysical truth.
Correction: The majority argue it is logically impossible because distinctness is simply the negation of identity.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, why is the statement 'neither identical nor distinct' improbable?
A) Because it contradicts revelation
B) Because it posits an intermediary between negation and affirmation
C) Because it is a foreign concept
D) Because it was never said by any scholar

Key Takeaway
The majority rejects any middle ground between identity and distinctness, as distinctness is simply the negation of identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
In your relationship with Allah, is there any area where you try to claim 'neither fully obedient nor fully disobedient,' thinking it exempts you from accountability? How does the logic of this majority’s objection force you to examine that gray area and repent?

When you consider the attributes of Allah—His knowledge, power, will—do you tend to think of them as somehow both identical and distinct from His essence, avoiding the hard choice? This lesson says that choice must be made. Which side will you commit to after truly understanding the argument?

The Verbal Dispute Defense
Al-Ījī presents and explains the verbal dispute defense of the controversial statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some scholars defend the statement 'neither identical nor distinct' by arguing that it is a verbal dispute based on a technical definition of 'distinct'.

Core Idea
The dispute is merely about the meaning of 'distinct', not about reality.

After presenting the majority's objection, al-Ījī turns to a defense offered by some scholars. They argue that the apparent contradiction is only a verbal dispute (nizā‘ lafẓī). Those who made the statement had a specific technical definition of 'al-ghayr' that requires separability, so on that definition, a thing may be neither identical nor distinct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defense
Read the part where some scholars excuse the statement by calling it a verbal dispute.
2
Understand the technical definition
The passage explains that these scholars define 'two distinct things' as those that can be separated. Under this definition, the attribute and qualified are not separable, so they are not 'distinct' in that sense, but they are also not identical.
3
Contrast with the ordinary meaning
Note that if 'distinct' is used in its ordinary sense, then everything is either identical or distinct. The disagreement is purely about terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( واعلم أن قولهم) أي قول مشايخنا في الصفة مع الموصوف وفي الجزء مع الكل: (لا هو ولا غيره، مما استبعده (الجمهور) جدا ( فإنه إثبات للواسطة بين النفي والإثبات؛ إذ الغيرية تساوي نفي العينية، فكل ما ليس بعين فهو غير كما أن كل ما هو غير فليس بعين. ( ومنهم من اعتذر عن ذلك ) بأنه نزاع لفظي ( لا تعلق له بأمر معنوي؛ وذلك أن هؤلاء خصصوا لفظ الغير بأن اصطلحوا على أن الغيرين ما يجوز الانفكاك بينهما. وعلى هذا فالشيء بالقياس إلى آخر، قد لا يكون عيناً ولا غيراً، وإذا أجري لفظ الغير على معناه المشهور بلا تخصيص فكل شيء بالقياس إلى آخر، إما عين وإما غير.
(And know that their statement) – i.e., the statement of our scholars concerning the attribute with the qualified and concerning the part with the whole: (It is neither identical nor distinct) – is greatly deemed improbable by (the majority), (for it is a positing of an intermediary between negation and affirmation. Since distinctness (al-ghayriyya) is equivalent to negating identity (al-‘ayniyya), everything that is not identical is distinct, just as everything that is distinct is not identical. (And some of them excused this) by [saying] that it is a verbal dispute (that has no connection to a conceptual matter). That is because these [scholars] specified the word "al-ghayr" by stipulating that "the two distinct things (al-ghayrān)" are those between which separation is permissible. On this [definition], a thing in relation to another may be neither identical nor distinct. But if the word "al-ghayr" is used according to its well-known meaning without specification, then everything in relation to another is either identical or distinct.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses the controversial statement attributed to some early scholars concerning the relationship between an attribute and its qualified, and between a part and the whole: they said "it is neither identical nor distinct" (lā huwa wa lā ghayruhu). The majority of theologians (al-jumhūr) strongly reject this because it posits a third category between identity and distinctness. The author explains that distinctness (al-ghayriyya) is simply the negation of identity (al-‘ayniyya), so everything that is not identical is distinct, and vice versa. There is no middle ground. However, some scholars defend this statement by arguing that it is a verbal dispute (nizā‘ lafẓī) with no real conceptual import. They claim that those who made the statement had a technical definition of "al-ghayr": they defined "the two distinct things" as those that can be separated. According to this technical usage, a thing can be neither identical nor distinct. But if the term is used in its ordinary sense, then everything is either identical or distinct. Thus the disagreement is merely about the definition of the word, not about any real metaphysical fact.

Key Terms
النزاع اللفظي
الغير
الانفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the defense is an admission of a real contradiction.
Correction: The defense shows that there is no real contradiction, only a difference in the definition of 'distinct'.

Checkpoint
According to the verbal dispute defense, when might a thing be neither identical nor distinct to another?
A) When they are partially identical
B) When 'distinct' is defined as 'that which can be separated'
C) When the majority is wrong
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction in 'neither identical nor distinct' is resolved by recognizing it is a verbal dispute over the definition of 'distinct'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with someone, do you immediately assume they are wrong, or do you first ask whether you mean the same thing by the key terms? How can this lesson transform your approach to future disputes?
?
Consider a recent conflict in your life. Was it possibly a verbal dispute—a disagreement over words rather than realities? How would you go back and clarify definitions to seek real understanding?
?
This lesson shows that the same statement can be true or false depending on how we define a word. Are you more attached to the words you use or to the truths they point to? Ask yourself honestly: would you rather win an argument or arrive at the truth?

Metaphorical Union: Transformation and Composition
Al-Iji defines two metaphorical senses of union with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two metaphorical meanings of union (itihad) as transformation and composition, and why both are possible and actually occur.

Core Idea
Union is real only in metaphor; true union of essences is impossible.

Before we can discuss whether union can occur between two things, we must clarify what 'union' means. Al-Iji distinguishes between a metaphorical sense, which is observable in the world, and a real sense that would imply a thing becoming another in its very self. This passage identifies two types of metaphorical union: transformation (like water becoming air) and composition (like dust becoming mud). Both are permissible and indeed occur.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of union
Start by reading the Arabic passage that defines 'itihad' and its metaphorical applications. Notice how the author introduces the term and immediately gives examples.
The passage begins with 'الاتحاد يطلق بطريق المجاز على صيرورة شيء ما شيئاً آخر'
2
Identify the two metaphorical meanings
Look for the two modes: transformation (استحالة) and composition (تركيب). Trace how each is described and what examples are given.
Transformation involves change of form; composition involves joining of parts.
3
Reflect on the possibility of metaphorical union
The author states that union in these two senses is 'undoubtedly permissible, indeed it actually occurs'. Ask yourself: why is it important to establish that metaphorical union is possible?
This sets the stage for contrasting with the real concept, which will be shown impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : الاثنان لا يتحدان وهذا حكم ضروري (الاثنان لا يتحدان)، الاتحاد يطلق بطريق المجاز على صيرورة شيء ما شيئاً آخر، بطريق الاستحالة أعني التغير والانتقال دفعياً كان أو تدريجياً، كما يقال : صار الماء هواء، والأسود أبيض، ففي الأول زال حقيقة الماء بزوال صورته النوعية عن هيولاه، وانضم إلى تلك الهيولى الصورة النوعية التي للهواء فحصل حقيقة أخرى حقيقة الهواء. وفي الثاني زال صفة السواد عن الموصوف بها، واتصف بصفة أخرى هي البياض، ويطلق أيضاً بطريق المجاز على صيرورة شيء شيئاً آخر بطريق التركيب، وهو أن ينضم شيء إلى شيء ثان فيحصل منهما شيء ثالث، كما يقال : صار التراب طيناً، والخشب سريراً، والاتحاد بهذين المعنيين لا شك في جوازه، بل في وقوعه أيضاً، وأما المفهوم الحقيقي للاتحاد فهو : أن يصير شيء بعينه شيئاً آخر، ومعنى قولنا : بعينه أنه صار شيئاً آخر من غير أن يزول عنه شيء أو ينضم إليه شيء، وإنما كان هذا مفهوماً حقيقياً؛ لأنه المتبادر من الاتحاد عند الإطلاق. وإنما يتصور هذا المعنى الحقيقي على وجهين الأول أن يكون هناك شيئان كزيد وعمرو مثلاً، فيتحدان بأن يصير زيد عمراً، أو بالعكس. ففي هذا الوجه قبل الاتحاد شيئان، وبعده شيء واحد كان حاصلا قبله، والثاني أن يكون هناك شيء واحد، كزيد فيصير هو شخصاً آخر غيره، فحينئذ يكون قبل الاتحاد أمر واحد، وبعده أمر آخر لم يكن حاصلا قبله بل بعده. وهذا المعنى الحقيقي باطل بالضرورة، وإليه أشار بقوله : (هذا) أي عدم اتحاد الاثنين حكم ضروري يحكم به بديهة العقل بعد تجريد الطرفين على ما ينبغي. فإن الاختلاف والتغاير بين الماهيتين، وبين الهويتين، وكذا بين الماهية والهوية اختلاف وتغاير بالذات، فلا يعقل زواله، يعني التغاير بين كل اثنين فرضاً مقتضى ذاتهما، فلا يمكن زواله عنهما كسائر لوازم الماهيات. (وهذا) الحكم مع وضوحه في نفسه ربما يزاد توضيحه بنوع تنبيه.
The fourth station - the eighth objective: Two things do not unite, and this is a necessary judgment. (Two things do not unite). 'Itihad' is applied metaphorically to something becoming another thing, through the mode of transformation, meaning change and transition, whether instantaneous or gradual, such as saying: water became air, and the black became white. In the first, the reality of water disappeared by the disappearance of its specific form from its matter, and the specific form of air joined that matter, so another reality, the reality of air, resulted. In the second, the attribute of blackness disappeared from its subject, and it was described by another attribute which is whiteness. It is also applied metaphorically to something becoming another thing through the mode of composition, which is that something joins a second thing and a third thing results from them, as is said: dust became mud, and wood became a bed. Union in these two meanings is undoubtedly permissible, indeed it actually occurs. As for the real concept of union, it is that a thing in its very self becomes another thing. The meaning of our saying 'in its very self' is that it becomes another thing without anything disappearing from it or anything joining it. This is a real concept because it is what is immediately understood from 'union' when unqualified. This real concept can be conceived in two ways: first, there are two things, like Zayd and Amr, and they unite by Zayd becoming Amr, or vice versa. In this way, before union there are two things, and after it one thing that existed before. Second, there is one thing, like Zayd, and he becomes another person distinct from him. In that case, before union there is one thing, and after it another thing that did not exist before but after. This real concept is false by necessity, and he indicated this by his saying: (this) meaning that the non-union of two things is a necessary judgment judged by the innate intellect after purifying the two sides appropriately. For the difference and distinction between the quiddities, and between the individualities, and also between the quiddity and individuality, is a difference and distinction by essence, so its removal is inconceivable. That is, the distinction between any two things assumed is a necessary concomitant of their essences, so its removal from them is impossible, like other necessities of quiddities. (This) judgment, despite its clarity in itself, may be further clarified by a kind of reminder.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It first defines 'itihad' (union) in two metaphorical senses: transformation and composition. Both are possible and occur. Then it defines the real sense: a thing becoming another thing in its very self without any change or composition. This real sense is what is intuitively understood by 'union'. The author then explains that this real union can be conceived in two ways: either two things become one (one of them turning into the other) or one thing becomes another distinct thing. Both are impossible by necessity. The proof is that the difference between any two things (whether quiddities or individualities) is essential, so it cannot be removed. This is a necessary judgment of reason. The commentary adds that this judgment is clear but can be further clarified.

Key Terms
اتحاد
مجاز
استحالة
تركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'union' in everyday language (e.g., water becoming air) is a real union of essences.
Correction: Such changes are merely metaphorical union; the water's essence does not literally become air's essence—only the form changes while the matter persists.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of metaphorical union through composition?
A) Water becoming air
B) Black becoming white
C) Dust becoming mud
D) Zayd becoming Amr

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical union (transformation or composition) is possible and occurs, but it is not true union of essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own personal changes—like becoming more patient or knowledgeable—do you see them as a transformation of your attributes or as a fundamental change in your essence? How does this distinction affect the way you take responsibility for your growth?
?
Think of a relationship you consider 'united' (e.g., with a spouse, a close friend, or even with God). In light of this lesson, is that union real or metaphorical? What would change if you treated it as a composition of two distinct beings rather than a fusion?
?
Have you ever tried to lose yourself in something—a hobby, a person, or an ideology—hoping to become one with it? How does the passage's insistence that real union is impossible challenge that desire, and what might be a healthier way to relate to what you love?

The Real Concept of Union and Its Impossibility
Al-Iji defines real union and proves its impossibility by necessary judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the real concept of union (itihad haqiqi) and why it is impossible by necessity, based on the essential distinction between any two things.

Core Idea
Real union would require a thing to become another in its very self—impossible by rational necessity.

Having clarified the permissible metaphorical senses of union, Al-Iji now turns to what is truly meant by 'union' when taken literally: a thing becoming another thing in its very self, without any change or composition. He then shows that this real union can only be conceived in two ways, both of which are impossible by necessity. The reason is that the difference between any two things (whether quiddities or individualities) is essential to them, so it cannot be removed. This passage grounds the impossibility of real union as a necessary judgment of the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of real union
Focus on the phrase 'أن يصير شيء بعينه شيئاً آخر' and its explanation. Understand what 'in its very self' means—no loss or gain of anything.
The author says this is what is immediately understood from 'union' when unqualified.
2
Identify the two conceivable ways of real union
The passage presents two ways: either two things become one (one turning into the other) or one thing becomes another distinct thing. Trace each scenario carefully.
3
Grasp the proof of impossibility
Read the reasoning: difference and distinction between quiddities and individualities is essential (by essence), so its removal is inconceivable. Why would this make real union impossible?
The proof relies on the principle that essential properties cannot be removed.
4
Connect to the lesson's core idea
Reflect on why this matters: it establishes a fundamental limit on what can be said about unity and multiplicity in metaphysics.
This is a foundational principle in kalam and philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Threefold Classification of Pairs
Al-Iji enumerates the three types of pairs and defines essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational divisions of any two things according to the mutakallimun and the definition of essential attributes.

Core Idea
Every pair of existents is either similar, opposite, or disparate.

Before we delve into detailed theological arguments, we need a clear vocabulary for comparing two things. The mutakallimun classified all pairs into three exhaustive categories based on whether they share essential attributes or how they can coexist. This passage lays out that foundational classification and defines what an essential attribute is.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification statement
Start by reading the first sentence which introduces the three divisions. Notice the conditional structure: if share attributes → similars; if impossible to coexist → opposites; otherwise → disparate.
The logic is exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
2
Examine the definition of similars
Similars are defined as two existents that share all essential attributes. Focus on the word 'all'—any difference in essential attributes breaks similarity.
3
Understand essential attributes
Read the definition of essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya). They are attributes derived from the thing itself without reference to anything external. Contrast with meaningful attributes (sifat ma'nawiyya) which require external consideration.
Example: animality is essential for a human; spatiality is meaningful.
4
Reflect on the completeness of the classification
Notice that the three categories cover all possibilities: either attributes shared or not, and if not, either coexistence impossible or possible. This is a logical division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد التاسع : الاثنان من المتكلمين ثلاثة أقسام. الاثنان عند أهل الحق من المتكلمين ثلاثة أقسام، لأنهما إن اشتركا في الصفات النفسية فالمثلان وإلا فإن امتنع لذاتيهما اجتماعهما في محل واحد من جهة واحدة فالضدان وإلا فالمتخالفان. أحدها المثلان وهما: الموجودان المشتركان في جميع الصفات النفسية. والمراد بالصفات النفسية ما لا يحتاج في وصف الشيء به إلى تعقل أمر خارج عن نفس ذلك الشيء، بأن يكون منتزعاً من نفسه، أو من جزئه كالحيوانية للإنسان. فما لا تكون منتزعاً من نفس الشيء صفة معنوية سواء كانت موجودة كالتحيز، أو معدومة كالحدوث. وبما حررنا لك اندفع التحيز الذي عرض لبعض الناظرين حيث قال : لا يخفى أن الظاهر من هذه العبارة أن تكون قوله : (ثلاثة أقسام انحصار الاثنين في الثلاثة مبني على أن لا تعدد بين المعدومين، ولا بين معدوم وموجود ، إذ لو ثبت التعدد بينهما لكانا اثنين مع عدم اندراجهما في شيء من الأقسام الثلاثة؛ لأن كلاً من الثلاثة موجودان على تفسيره. اللهم إلا أن يقال : التعدد لا يستلزم الاثنينية، وفيه بعد لا يخفى، ولكن لا مشاحة . قوله : ( في جميع الصفات النفسية قيل : ثبوت التماثل على هذا التقدير يتوقف على تحقق الاشتراك في جميع الصفات النفسية، ومن جملتها التماثل على ما صرح به بعيد، هذا فيتوقف التماثل على نفسه وأجيب تارة بتخصيص الصفات بغير التماثل وأخرى بأن التماثل يتوقف على التماثل، لا باعتبار أنه تماثل بل باعتبار أنه من الصفات النفسية، فيختلف العنوان، ويندفع الدور.
The Ninth Objective: Two things among the mutakallimun are three divisions. Two things according to the people of truth among the mutakallimun are three divisions: because if they share in the essential attributes, then they are similars; otherwise, if it is impossible for their essences to coexist in one locus from one aspect, then they are opposites; otherwise, they are disparate. One of them is similars, and they are: the two existents that share in all essential attributes. And what is meant by essential attributes is that which does not require, in describing a thing by it, conceiving something external to the essence of that thing, but is derived from itself or from its part, like animality for man. So what is not derived from the essence of the thing is a meaningful attribute, whether it is existent like spatiality or non-existent like temporality. And by what we have clarified for you, the confusion that occurred to some examiners is repelled, when he said: 'It is not hidden that the apparent from this expression is that it be...' His saying: ('three divisions') The confinement of two things to the three is based on there being no multiplicity between non-existents, nor between a non-existent and an existent; for if multiplicity were established between them, they would be two without falling under any of the three divisions, because each of the three are existents according to his interpretation. Unless it is said: multiplicity does not entail twoness, and there is remoteness in that, but no harm. His saying: ('in all essential attributes') It was said: The establishment of similarity on this estimation depends on the realization of sharing in all essential attributes, and among them is similarity as he explicitly stated – this is remote; thus similarity depends on itself. And it was answered sometimes by specifying the attributes to other than similarity, and other times by saying that similarity depends on similarity not insofar as it is similarity but insofar as it is among the essential attributes, so the title differs, and the circularity is repelled.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of pairs into three categories according to the mutakallimun. It begins by stating that two things, according to Ahl al-Haqq, are of three divisions: (1) similars (mithlan) if they share in all essential attributes; (2) opposites (diddan) if it is impossible for their essences to coexist in one locus from one aspect; (3) disparate (mukhtalifan) otherwise. The definition of similars is given as two existents that share in all essential attributes. Essential attributes are defined as those that do not require conceiving something external to the thing's essence; they are derived from the essence itself or its part, like animality for man. Any attribute not so derived is a 'meaningful attribute' (sifa ma'nawiyya). The commentator then addresses a potential confusion: some might think the definition implies that non-existents cannot be paired, but the classification is only for existents. He also discusses a circularity objection regarding similarity itself being among the essential attributes, and provides two solutions: restricting the attributes to non-similarity, or distinguishing the title of similarity as an essential attribute from the fact of similarity.

Key Terms
مثلان (similars)
ضدان (opposites)
متخالفان (disparates)
صفات نفسية (essential attributes)
معنوية (meaningful attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all pairs are either similar or opposite, neglecting the third category of disparate things.
Correction: The classification explicitly includes a third category (disparates) for pairs that are neither similar nor opposite, such as a human and a stone.

Checkpoint
What determines whether two things are opposites (ضدان)?
A) They share all essential attributes
B) They are both non-existent
C) It is impossible for their essences to coexist in one locus from one aspect
D) They are both existents that share no essential attributes

Key Takeaway
The threefold classification of pairs into similars, opposites, and disparates, based on essential attributes, is a fundamental tool for kalam reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a person you often compare yourself with. According to al-Iji's classification, are you 'similars', 'opposites', or 'disparates'? What does this teach you about the validity of your comparison? Will you stop assuming similarity or opposition where none truly exists?
?
Identify one attribute of yourself that you treat as essential (defining who you are) but which, according to al-Iji, is actually a meaningful attribute derived from external circumstances (e.g., success, family, or health). How can you re-evaluate your identity in light of this distinction?
?
Reflect on a situation where you tried to reconcile two opposing values or desires within yourself (e.g., ambition and contentment). Al-Iji says opposites cannot coexist in one locus. What must you change—not just balance—to resolve this opposition?

The Objection About Non-Existents
Al-Iji raises and resolves an objection about non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the threefold classification apparently excludes non-existent things and why the classification only applies to existents.

Core Idea
The three divisions apply only to existents, not to non-existents.

After defining the three categories, a subtle objection arises: what about pairs involving non-existents? They seem to be excluded from the classification. The text addresses this by clarifying that the classification is specifically for existents, and that multiplicity among non-existents does not constitute a valid pair in the relevant sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that the classification into three categories assumes no multiplicity between non-existents or between a non-existent and an existent. Otherwise, such pairs would fall outside the threefold division because all three categories require both to be existents.
The objector is concerned about the completeness of the classification.
2
Understand the response
The response suggests that multiplicity (taddud) does not necessarily imply twoness (ithnayniyya) in the sense required for classification. The discussion is about 'two things' as understood by the mutakallimun, which presumes existence.
The phrase 'لا مشاحة' indicates this is a terminological issue.
3
Reflect on the principle
The classification is for existents only. Non-existents or mixed pairs are not considered 'two things' in this technical context.
This preserves the exhaustiveness of the three categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Circularity Objection and Its Solutions
Al-Iji presents a circularity objection regarding similarity and offers two resolutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection that similarity depends on itself if it is an essential attribute, and the two solutions proposed to avoid the circle.

Core Idea
Similarity cannot be defined by itself; the circle is broken by either excluding similarity from essential attributes or by distinguishing the title.

A deeper logical problem arises: if similarity (tamathul) itself is an essential attribute, then defining similars as sharing all essential attributes would involve a circle—similarity would depend on itself. The text presents two ways out: either restrict essential attributes to those other than similarity, or argue that similarity as an essential attribute is a different concept from the relation of similarity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the circularity objection
The objection states: Since similarity is one of the essential attributes, then to be similar, two things must share all essential attributes including similarity itself. This creates a vicious circle: similarity depends on itself.
This is a logical problem of self-reference.
2
Examine the first solution
First solution: Specify that 'all essential attributes' excludes similarity itself. So similars share all essential attributes except similarity.
This avoids including the attribute being defined.
3
Examine the second solution
Second solution: Distinguish between similarity as a conceptual relation and similarity as an essential attribute (the property of being similar to another). The circle is broken because the dependence is not on the same aspect.
The 'title differs' so there is no circularity.
4
Reflect on the importance
This shows the mutakallimun's precision in handling definitional circularity, which is a common issue in ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What is one of the two solutions proposed to avoid the circularity in defining similars?

?

?
The passage shows that a subtle distinction in perspective (similarity as relation vs. essential attribute) can resolve a logical deadlock. Where in your life have you been trapped in a vicious cycle—perhaps in justifying a sin or in a relationship—that might be broken by shifting how you view the issue? What 'title' change could you make right now, and what will you do about it by the end of today?
?

Could you eliminate something from your set of 'essential' criteria, or could you see it under a different description? Which path feels more honest, and why are you delaying it?

Definition of Tamāthul and Its Essential Nature
Defines tamāthul using the three rational judgments and argues it is a nafsī attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how tamāthul is defined using the three rational judgments and why it is considered an essential attribute (nafsī) rather than a qualitative one.

Core Idea
Tamāthul is essential to its subject, not an added attribute.

Before discussing similarity and difference among created things, theologians must define what it means for two things to be alike. This passage from Sanusi’s work clarifies that tamāthul is not a mere relational property; it is rooted in the very essence of a thing. By examining the definitions and the argument that follows, you will see why this matters for foundational theology.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
Read the definitions of al-ṣifa al-nafsiyya and al-ma‘nawiyya in the first line. Understand that the nafsī attribute cannot be imagined absent.
The contrast sets up the argument for tamāthul.
2
Understand the definition of tamāthul
The passage gives two formulations of the definition. Identify how they invoke the three rational judgments (necessary, possible, impossible).
These judgments are the basis for similarity.
3
Follow the argument that tamāthul is nafsī
Notice the reasoning linking tamāthul to the essence itself without an added meaning. This establishes its status as an essential attribute.
The conclusion is that tamāthul is not a relational attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة النفسية ما لا يصح توهم ارتفاعها عن موصوفها والمعنوية ما يقابلها. ويلزمها المشاركة فيما يجب ويمكن ويمتنع، ولذلك قد يعرف به فيقال: المثلان هما الموجودان اللذان يشارك كل منهما الآخر فيما يجب له ويمكن ويمتنع. وقد يقال: بعبارة أخرى المثلان: ما يسد أحدهما مسد الآخر في الأحكام الواجبة والجائزة والممتنعة جميعاً، ولأن الصفة النفسية كما عرفت ما يعود إلى نفس الذات لا إلى معنى زائد على الذات، فالتماثل من الصفات النفسية، لأنه أمر ذاتي ليس لمعنى زائد.
The essential attribute is that whose imagined removal from its subject is not valid, and the qualitative attribute is its opposite. And it necessitates participation in what is necessary, possible, and impossible. Therefore it may be defined by it, and it is said: The two similars are the two existents each of which shares with the other in what is necessary for it, possible for it, and impossible for it. And it may be said in other words: The two similars are that which one takes the place of the other in all obligatory, permissible, and impossible judgments. And because the essential attribute, as you have known, is that which goes back to the essence itself, not to an added meaning upon the essence, so tamāthul is among the essential attributes, because it is a self-subsistent matter not due to an added meaning.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of tamāthul (likeness) among the theologians. It first defines an essential (nafsī) attribute as one whose imagined absence from its subject is impossible, as opposed to a qualitative (ma‘nawiyya) attribute which can be imagined absent. It then states that participation in essential attributes necessarily entails participation in what is necessary, possible, and impossible for the essence. Therefore, one definition of two similars is that each shares with the other in its necessary, possible, and impossible attributes. An alternative definition is that one can substitute for the other in all necessary, permissible, and impossible judgments. The passage then argues that tamāthul itself is an essential attribute because it pertains to the essence itself without any added meaning. The reasoning is: since the essential attribute is defined as that which refers to the essence itself and not to an added meaning, and tamāthul is a matter of the essence itself, it follows that tamāthul is an essential attribute. This establishes the foundation for understanding sameness and difference in kalam.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think tamāthul is a relational attribute that depends on other entities.
Correction: The passage argues that tamāthul is an essential (nafsī) attribute because it pertains to the essence itself, not to an added meaning; thus it is not dependent on relations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of two similars (al-mithlān)?
A) Two existents that share in all essential attributes.
B) Two existents that each share with the other in what is necessary, possible, and impossible for it.
C) Two existents that have the same essence.
D) Two existents that are identical.

Key Takeaway
Tamāthul is defined by sharing in necessary, possible, and impossible attributes and is an essential attribute of the subject itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at yourself, do you base your identity on what is essential to your humanness or on added labels and achievements? How would seeing your own essence as unchangeable change the way you pursue growth?
?
If tamāthul means sharing in necessary, possible, and impossible attributes, what does that imply about how you judge others? Are you willing to grant that they share your same essential nature, or do you treat them as fundamentally different?
?
The passage says tamāthul is not an added meaning. What added meanings do you attach to people—race, status, nationality—that prevent you from recognizing their essential likeness to you? What would it cost you to drop those additions?

What Is Kalam?

The Ash'arite Critique of Defining Similars
Ash'arite objection to defining similars by the most specific attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite argument against defining similars via the most specific attribute of the self and why necessary similarity cannot be caused.

Core Idea
If similarity is necessary, it cannot be caused—undermining definitions via shared attributes.

This passage opens with the Ash'arite objection: if two things are similar because they share the most specific attribute of their essence, then when that attribute differs, the similarity would be caused by different causes. This leads to a deeper critique: if similarity is necessary for similars, it cannot be caused, which contradicts the notion of defining similars by an attribute that would cause similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three divisions.
The passage begins by stating that 'the two among theologians are three divisions.' Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the context.
This sets up the discussion of similars.
2
Follow the argument about the specific attribute.
Notice the conditional: if the more specific attribute is different, then the total of attributes of the self differs, so similarity would be caused by different causes. Understand why this is problematic.
3
Consider the necessity of similarity.
The passage argues that similarity for similars is either necessary or not. If necessary, it cannot be caused according to Ash'arite principles. Reflect on why this prevents defining God by shared attributes.
This connects to the impossibility of causing necessary attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد التاسع : الاثنان من المتكلمين ثلاثة أقسام
فإن الأخص إذا كان مختلفاً كان مجموع صفات النفس بين السوادين مخالفاً لمجموعها في البياض، فيكون التماثل المعلل بالمجموع معللاً بعلل مختلفة. والقائلون بالحال من الأشاعرة لا يجوزونه أيضاً . وأيضاً فالتماثل للمثلين، إما واجب فلا يعلل التماثل حينئذ على رأيهم، إذ من قواعدهم أن الصفة الواجبة يمتنع تعليلها، ومن ثمة قالوا : لما كان عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لذاته امتنع أن تكون معللة بالعلم، فلا يجوز تعريفه بالاشتراك في أخص صفات النفس، لاقتضائه أن يكون التماثل معللاً بالأخص كما مر أولاً يكون واجباً للمثلين فيجوز حينئذ كون السوادين مختلفين تارة وغير مختلفين أخرى بأن يثبت لهما التماثل فيكونان متماثلين ويزول عنهما فيكونان مختلفين وبطلانه ظاهر. وقال النجار من المعتزلة : المثلان هما المشتركان في صفة إثبات وليس أحدهما بالثاني قيد الصفة بالثبوتية؛ لأن الاشتراك في الصفات السلبية لا يوجب التماثل، ويلزمه السواد والبياض، فإنهما مشتركان في صفات ثبوتية كالعرضية واللونية والحدوث، ويلزمه أيضاً مماثلة الرب للمربوب ؛ إذ يشتركان في بعض الصفات الثبوتية كالعالمية والقادرية، فإن قلت لعله أراد أن المشتركين في صفة وجودية متماثلان لا مطلقاً، بل في تلك الصفة. وحينئذ يلزمه أن السواد والبياض متماثلان في اللونية مثلا
قوله : إما واجب أي واجب الحصول لموصوفه عند حصول الموصوف .
قوله : فيكون التماثل المعلل بالمجموع إلخ لا يخفى أن من جملة صفة النفس التماثل، فلا بد أن يراد مجموع ما عداه فإن قلت : تعليل التماثل بمجموع صفات النفس يناقض ظاهر ما سبق، من أنه لا نفس الذوات . قلت : مراده من كونه لا نفس الذوات أنه ليس معللاً بأمر زائد عليها، كما صرح به هناك. والصفات النفسية ليست زائدة عليها فلا تناقض . قوله : إما واجب فلا يعلل قيل : تعليل الواجب بذات الموصوف جائز عندهم كالجوهرية بذات الجوهر والمحال تعليله بصفة عارضة . فهذا الاعتراض إنما يرد عليهم إذا قالوا بزيادة ذلك الأخص، وجوابه المنع ؛ فإن الظاهر أن الجواب عندهم لا يعقل أصلا يدل على ذلك كلامهم في المقصد العاشر من مرصد العلة والمعلول.
قوله : ويلزمه أيضاً مماثلة الرب فيه نظر لجواز أن يريد بقوله : وليس أحدهما بالثاني، وليس أحدهما بسبب الثاني فلا يلزم مماثلة الرب للمربوب . نعم لو لم يحمل عليه لم يلزم الاستغناء عنه، كما ظن لجواز أن يحمل على أن ليس أحدهما ثانياً، ليخرج الفصل مع النوع والجنس؛ لأن أحد هذه الثلاثة هو الآخر إذ يحمل عليه . اللهم إلا أن يقال : المراد الموجودان، ولا وجود إلا للاشخاص، وقيل : المراد ليس أحدهما قائماً بالثاني ليخرج الصفة مع الموصوف .
قلت : فيلزم أن يكون الباري مماثلاً للمخلوقين في بعض الأشياء، مع أنه لم يجوز كونه تعالى مماثلا للحوادث أصلا .
قوله : مع أنه لم يجوز كونه إلخ على صيغة المجهول كما يدل عليه قوله : فيلزم لقوله تعالى : ليس كمثله شيء ، وفيه : أن نفي المماثلة عنه تعالى إما باعتبار أنه لا اشتراك بينه الممكنات إلا في اللفظ وإما باعتبار أن المراد الاتحاد في الماهية. وهذا لا ينافي كونه مماثلاً لها في بعض العوارض، وأما عدم الإطلاق فلرعاية التأدب ودفع التوهم . واعلم أن هذا السؤال والجواب بعد ملاحظة ما سيجيء من قول المصنف : وعليه يحمل قول النجار تكرار إلا أن يقال : إنه أورده الشارح قدس سره هاهنا لبعد العهد
The Fourth Observatory - The Ninth Intention: The Two among the theologians are three divisions. For if the more specific [attribute] is different, then the total of the attributes of the self (ṣifāt al-nafs) between the two blacknesses would be different from its total in whiteness, so the similarity (al-tamāthul) caused by the total would be caused by different causes. And those who affirm the state (al-ḥāl) among the Ashʿarites also do not permit it. Also, similarity for the two similars (al-mithlān): either it is necessary (wājib), then it is not caused; similarity then (according to their view), for it is among their principles that the necessary attribute cannot be caused. Hence they said: Since the omniscience of God most high is necessary by His essence, it is impossible that it be caused by knowledge, so it is not permissible to define Him by sharing in the most specific attributes of the self, because it would require that similarity be caused by the most specific, as was mentioned. First, it is necessary for the two similars; then it is possible that the two blacknesses be sometimes different and sometimes not different, by establishing similarity for them so they become similar, and then it disappears from them so they become different; and its falsehood is apparent. And al-Najjār from the Muʿtazila said: The two similars are the two that share in a positive attribute (ṣifa ithbātiyya) and neither of them is by the other. He restricted the attribute to positive, because sharing in negative attributes does not necessitate similarity. And it follows from him that blackness and whiteness are similar, for they share in positive attributes like accidence (al-ʿaraḍiyya), color (al-lawniyya), and temporal origination (al-ḥudūth). And it also follows from him the similarity of the Lord to the creature, for they share in some positive attributes like omniscience and omnipotence. If you say: Perhaps he meant that the two sharing in an existential attribute are similar, not absolutely, but in that attribute. Then it follows from him that blackness and whiteness are similar in color, for example. His saying: 'either necessary' means necessary of attainment for its described thing at the attainment of the described thing. His saying: 'so similarity caused by the total etc.' it is not hidden that among the attributes of the self is similarity, so it must be intended as the total of what is other than it. If you say: Causing similarity by the total of the attributes of the self contradicts the apparent meaning of what preceded, that it is not the same as the essences. I say: His meaning in being not the same as the essences is that it is not caused by something additional to them, as he stated there. And the attributes of the self are not additional to them, so there is no contradiction. His saying: 'either necessary then it is not caused.' It was said: Causing the necessary by the essence of the described is permissible according to them, like substantiality by the essence of the substance, and it is impossible to cause it by an accidental attribute. So this objection only applies to them if they affirm the addition of that more specific [attribute], and its answer is denial; for it is apparent that the answer according to them is not rationally understood at all, as indicated by their speech in the Tenth Intention of the Observatory of Cause and Effect. His saying: 'and it also follows from him the similarity of the Lord' ... it deserves consideration, because it is permissible that he meant by his saying 'and neither of them is by the other' that neither of them is due to the other, so the similarity of the Lord to the creature would not follow. Indeed, if it were not interpreted that way, the independence from it would not follow, as thought, because it is permissible to interpret it as neither of them is a second, to exclude the differentia with the species and the genus, because one of these three is the other when predicated. Unless it is said: The intended are existents, and existence is only for individuals. And it was said: The intended is that neither of them subsists in the other, to exclude the attribute with the described. I say: Then it would follow that the Creator is similar to creatures in some things, even though He is not considered similar to temporal things at all. His saying: 'even though He is not considered etc.' in the passive voice, as indicated by his saying: 'then it would follow' due to His saying most high: 'There is nothing like Him' (Qur'an 42:11). And in it, that the negation of similarity from Him most high is either in the sense that there is no sharing between Him and possible things except in wording, or in the sense that unity in quiddity is intended. And this does not contradict His being similar to them in some accidents. As for the lack of absolute expression, it is for observing propriety and repelling misconception. And know that this question and answer, after considering what will come from the author's saying 'and upon it is interpreted the saying of al-Najjār', is repetition, unless it is said that the commentator, may God sanctify his secret, mentioned it here because of the distance of the era.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanatory discussion on the definition of similars (al-mithlān) within the framework of the three divisions of 'the two' among theologians. It begins by presenting the Ashʿarite objection to defining similars based on the most specific attribute of the self (aṣafāt al-nafs), arguing that if similarity were caused by the total of attributes, it would be caused by different causes when the specific attribute differs. Then it addresses the possibility that similarity is necessary, which would preclude causation, as necessary attributes cannot be caused. This leads to rejecting the definition of God through shared attributes, as that would require caused similarity. The passage then introduces al-Najjār's definition: similars are two things sharing a positive attribute, with neither being the other. It notes the restriction to positive attributes to exclude negative ones. The commentary then examines implications: blackness and whiteness would be similar due to shared positive attributes like accidence and color, and worse, the Lord would be similar to the creature due to shared attributes like omniscience. The commentator considers possible interpretations to avoid this consequence, such as interpreting 'not by each other' as not being caused by each other, or as not being numerically second, or as not subsisting in each other. Then a further objection is raised: under some interpretations, God would still be similar to creatures in some respects, contradicting explicit scripture. The response clarifies that the negation of similarity in the Qur'an can be understood as denying essential unity, not accidental similarity, and that avoiding absolute expression is for propriety. The passage concludes with a note that this discussion is a repetition of what will come later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similars can be defined by sharing the most specific attribute of the self.
Correction: The Ash'arites argue that this leads to contradiction because similarity would have to be caused, but if it's necessary it cannot be caused.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite argument, why can similarity not be caused if it is necessary?
A) Because necessary attributes are eternal and uncaused.
B) Because similarity is accidental and cannot be necessary.
C) Because the most specific attribute is not real.
D) Because the cause would be external.

Key Takeaway
Ash'arites argue that defining similars by the most specific attribute fails because necessary similarity cannot be caused, and the definition would imply variability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God's attributes as qualities that He 'has' in a way similar to how creatures possess attributes? This passage argues that God's necessary attributes cannot be caused. Does your understanding of God's omniscience treat it as inherent and uncaused, or do you sometimes imagine it as acquired? How might that affect your trust in His unchanging knowledge?
?
When you reflect on your own beliefs about God, are there any attributes you tend to define or understand by comparing them to created things? This passage warns against defining God by shared attributes. In what specific area of your faith might you be inadvertently limiting God by drawing parallels to creation? What will you do to correct this?
?
The Ash'arites argue that if similarity were caused, it could vary, leading to absurdity. Think about your certainty in God's perfection. Is your conviction based on logical necessity or on subjective feelings? If your faith wavers, does that suggest you are treating God's attributes as if they could be otherwise? How can you anchor your belief in the necessary nature of His attributes, so that your trust does not fluctuate with circumstances?

Al-Najjār's Definition and Its Theological Problems
Al-Najjār's definition of similars, objections, and interpretive replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Najjār's definition of similars as sharing a positive attribute, the objections it raises (including divine similarity), and the attempted replies.

Core Idea
Al-Najjār's definition risks making God similar to creation—a theologically untenable consequence.

Having rejected the Ash'arite view, the passage now presents al-Najjār's definition of similars: two things sharing a positive attribute, with neither being the other. However, this definition immediately faces objections: it would imply that blackness and whiteness are similar, and worse, that the Lord and creatures are similar due to shared positive attributes like omniscience. The commentator then explores interpretations that might salvage the definition, including reinterpreting 'not by each other' to avoid the most egregious consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Najjār's definition and note the restriction to positive attributes.
Al-Najjār defines similars as two things sharing a positive attribute (ṣifa ithbātiyya) and neither being the other. Understand why he excludes negative attributes.
Positive attributes are those that affirm something about the thing.
2
Identify the two immediate objections.
First, blackness and whiteness share positive attributes like accidence and color, so they would be similar. Second, the Lord and creatures share attributes like omniscience, implying divine similarity. Trace how these follow.
These objections show the definition is too broad.
3
Examine the proposed reinterpretations.
The commentator suggests that 'not by each other' could mean 'not caused by each other' or 'not numerically identical' or 'not subsisting in each other'. Assess whether these avoid the objections.
Each reinterpretation tries to exclude problematic cases.
4
Consider the scriptural objection and response.
A further objection is that even under reinterpretation, God would still be similar in some respects, contradicting 'There is nothing like Him'. The response distinguishes essential unity from accidental similarity.
The response maintains that similarity in accidents is not meant by the verse.

The Fourth Observatory - The Ninth Intention: The Two among the theologians are three divisions. For if the more specific [attribute] is different, then the total of the attributes of the self (ṣifāt al-nafs) between the two blacknesses would be different from its total in whiteness, so the similarity (al-tamāthul) caused by the total would be caused by different causes. And those who affirm the state (al-ḥāl) among the Ashʿarites also do not permit it. Also, similarity for the two similars (al-mithlān): either it is necessary (wājib), then it is not caused; similarity then (according to their view), for it is among their principles that the necessary attribute cannot be caused. Hence they said: Since the omniscience of God most high is necessary by His essence, it is impossible that it be caused by knowledge, so it is not permissible to define Him by sharing in the most specific attributes of the self, because it would require that similarity be caused by the most specific, as was mentioned. First, it is necessary for the two similars; then it is possible that the two blacknesses be sometimes different and sometimes not different, by establishing similarity for them so they become similar, and then it disappears from them so they become different; and its falsehood is apparent. And al-Najjār from the Muʿtazila said: The two similars are the two that share in a positive attribute (ṣifa ithbātiyya) and neither of them is by the other. He restricted the attribute to positive, because sharing in negative attributes does not necessitate similarity. And it follows from him that blackness and whiteness are similar, for they share in positive attributes like accidence (al-ʿaraḍiyya), color (al-lawniyya), and temporal origination (al-ḥudūth). And it also follows from him the similarity of the Lord to the creature, for they share in some positive attributes like omniscience and omnipotence. If you say: Perhaps he meant that the two sharing in an existential attribute are similar, not absolutely, but in that attribute. Then it follows from him that blackness and whiteness are similar in color, for example. His saying: 'either necessary' means necessary of attainment for its described thing at the attainment of the described thing. His saying: 'so similarity caused by the total etc.' it is not hidden that among the attributes of the self is similarity, so it must be intended as the total of what is other than it. If you say: Causing similarity by the total of the attributes of the self contradicts the apparent meaning of what preceded, that it is not the same as the essences. I say: His meaning in being not the same as the essences is that it is not caused by something additional to them, as he stated there. And the attributes of the self are not additional to them, so there is no contradiction. His saying: 'either necessary then it is not caused.' It was said: Causing the necessary by the essence of the described is permissible according to them, like substantiality by the essence of the substance, and it is impossible to cause it by an accidental attribute. So this objection only applies to them if they affirm the addition of that more specific [attribute], and its answer is denial; for it is apparent that the answer according to them is not rationally understood at all, as indicated by their speech in the Tenth Intention of the Observatory of Cause and Effect. His saying: 'and it also follows from him the similarity of the Lord' ... it deserves consideration, because it is permissible that he meant by his saying 'and neither of them is by the other' that neither of them is due to the other, so the similarity of the Lord to the creature would not follow. Indeed, if it were not interpreted that way, the independence from it would not follow, as thought, because it is permissible to interpret it as neither of them is a second, to exclude the differentia with the species and the genus, because one of these three is the other when predicated. Unless it is said: The intended are existents, and existence is only for individuals. And it was said: The intended is that neither of them subsists in the other, to exclude the attribute with the described. I say: Then it would follow that the Creator is similar to creatures in some things, even though He is not considered similar to temporal things at all. His saying: 'even though He is not considered etc.' in the passive voice, as indicated by his saying: 'then it would follow' due to His saying most high: 'There is nothing like Him' (Qur'an 42:11). And in it, that the negation of similarity from Him most high is either in the sense that there is no sharing between Him and possible things except in wording, or in the sense that unity in quiddity is intended. And this does not contradict His being similar to them in some accidents. As for the lack of absolute expression, it is for observing propriety and repelling misconception. And know that this question and answer, after considering what will come from the author's saying 'and upon it is interpreted the saying of al-Najjār', is repetition, unless it is said that the commentator, may God sanctify his secret, mentioned it here because of the distance of the era.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanatory discussion on the definition of similars (al-mithlān) within the framework of the three divisions of 'the two' among theologians. It begins by presenting the Ashʿarite objection to defining similars based on the most specific attribute of the self (aṣafāt al-nafs), arguing that if similarity were caused by the total of attributes, it would be caused by different causes when the specific attribute differs. Then it addresses the possibility that similarity is necessary, which would preclude causation, as necessary attributes cannot be caused. This leads to rejecting the definition of God through shared attributes, as that would require caused similarity. The passage then introduces al-Najjār's definition: similars are two things sharing a positive attribute, with neither being the other. It notes the restriction to positive attributes to exclude negative ones. The commentary then examines implications: blackness and whiteness would be similar due to shared positive attributes like accidence and color, and worse, the Lord would be similar to the creature due to shared attributes like omniscience. The commentator considers possible interpretations to avoid this consequence, such as interpreting 'not by each other' as not being caused by each other, or as not being numerically second, or as not subsisting in each other. Then a further objection is raised: under some interpretations, God would still be similar to creatures in some respects, contradicting explicit scripture. The response clarifies that the negation of similarity in the Qur'an can be understood as denying essential unity, not accidental similarity, and that avoiding absolute expression is for propriety. The passage concludes with a note that this discussion is a repetition of what will come later.

Key Terms
النَّجَّار
صفة إثبات
الرب
المربوب
التماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similars can be defined by sharing any attribute, including negative ones.
Correction: Al-Najjār restricts to positive attributes, but even this leads to theological problems like divine similarity.

Checkpoint
What is al-Najjār's definition of al-mithlān (similars)?
A) Two things that share in any attribute, positive or negative.
B) Two things that share in a positive attribute and neither is the other.
C) Two things that are necessarily similar by essence.
D) Two things that share in the most specific attribute of the self.

Key Takeaway
Al-Najjār's definition of similars, while intended to be precise, raises serious theological objections, especially regarding divine similarity, and requires careful interpretive work to avoid heresy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah's attributes—like His knowledge or power—do you ever catch yourself unconsciously measuring them against human abilities? What would it cost you to truly believe that His omniscience is not 'more' but utterly different from any knowledge you have? Name one specific way you will guard your tongue this week from comparing Allah to His creation.
?
The commentators labored to protect Allah's transcendence from a definition that risked making Him like creatures. Is there a belief or habit in your own life that subtly diminishes Allah's uniqueness—perhaps treating His forgiveness like human forgiveness, or His provision like a salary? Will you let this lesson expose that area and repent from it today?
?
Al-Najjār's definition forced his followers to reinterpret words to avoid heresy. When the apparent meaning of a Quranic verse or a sound hadith seems to contradict tawhid, do you trust that the scholars' interpretations are necessary, or do you feel a resistance? How will you cultivate humility before the authoritative interpretations of Ahl al-Sunna?

What Is Kalam?
