Definition of Opposites (Mutaqabilan)
Al-Jurjani defines opposites and gives illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical definition of opposites as pairs that cannot coexist in a single subject, and how to apply this test to examples.

Core Idea
Opposites are pairs that cannot coexist in a single subject.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of opposites (mutaqabilan), a key term in logic and kalam. Al-Jurjani begins by defining what it means for two things to be opposites and gives a crucial example to illustrate when two things are not opposites, setting the stage for the classification that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the key phrase 'لا يجتمعان تبايناً' (do not coexist in terms of differentiation).
The word 'tabayun' is technical; it means mutual exclusion.
2
Consider the non-example
Why are 'man' and 'horse' not opposites? Because they can coexist in the world (different subjects).
This highlights that opposites require impossibility of coexistence in a single subject.
3
Examine the example of whiteness and non-whiteness
Whiteness and non-whiteness cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time, making them true opposites.
This is an example of the category of negation and affirmation.
4
Understand the analogy with sight and blindness
The analogy reinforces that coexistence is impossible by virtue of inherence (hulul) in the same subject.
Sight and blindness are a classic example of privation and disposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان تبايناً، فلا يدخل نحو الإنسان والفرس في تعريف المتقابلين، بخلاف مفهومي البياض واللابياض فإنه يمتنع اجتماعهما باعتبار الحلول في محل واحد، على قياس البصر والعمى الثاني : المشهور في تقسيم المتقابلين أنهما إما وجوديان أو لا، وعلى الأول إما أن يكون تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى الآخر، فهما المتضايفان أو لا، فهما المتضادان . وعلى الثاني يكون أحدهما وجودياً، والآخر عدمياً، فإما أن يعتبر في العدمي محل قابل للوجودي، فهما العدم والملكة أو لا فهما السلب والإيجاب.
قوله : ( على قياس البصر والعمى فإن امتناع الاجتماع بينهما باعتبار الحلول أظهر، لكون المحل القابل معتبراً في العدمي .
قوله : ( وجوديان ) أي ليس السلب داخلاً في مفهوم شيء منهما.
The fourth observation - the eleventh chapter: The two opposites (al-mutaqabilan) are two things that do not coexist in terms of differentiation, so things like man and horse are not included in the definition of opposites, unlike the concepts of whiteness and non-whiteness, for their coexistence is impossible by virtue of inherence in a single subject, analogous to sight and blindness. Second: The well-known classification of opposites is that they are either existential or not. In the first case, either each is conceived in relation to the other, so they are correlatives (al-mutadayifan), or not, so they are contraries (al-mutadaddan). In the second case, one is existential and the other is privative. Then either a subject capable of the existential is considered in the privative, so they are privation and disposition (al-‘adm wa al-malaka), or not, so they are negation and affirmation (al-salb wa al-ijab).
His saying: (on the analogy of sight and blindness) for the impossibility of their coexistence by virtue of inherence is more apparent, because the capable subject is considered in the privative.
His saying: (existential) i.e., negation is not included in the concept of either of them.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the logical concept of opposites (mutaqabilan). It begins by stating that opposites are two things that cannot coexist in terms of differentiation (tabayun), meaning they cannot both be true of the same subject at the same time. The author excludes examples like 'man' and 'horse' because they can coexist in a general sense (they are not opposites). Instead, he gives the example of whiteness and non-whiteness, which cannot coexist in the same subject. He then notes that the classification of opposites is well-known: they are either both existential (such that negation is not part of their concept) or not. If existential, either each is understood relative to the other—these are correlatives (al-mutadayifan)—or not—these are contraries (al-mutadaddan). If one is existential and the other privative, then if the privative considers a subject capable of the existential, they are privation and disposition (al-‘adm wa al-malaka); otherwise, they are negation and affirmation (al-salb wa al-ijab). The glosses clarify: the analogy of sight and blindness shows the impossibility more clearly because the capable subject is considered in the privative. And 'existential' means negation is not part of the concept of either term.

Key Terms
المتقابلان
تبايناً
الحلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any two different things are opposites.
Correction: Opposites require impossibility of coexistence in the same subject at the same time. For example, man and horse are different but can coexist, so they are not opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, which of the following is NOT an example of opposites?
A) Whiteness and non-whiteness
B) Sight and blindness
C) Man and horse
D) Fatherhood and sonship

Key Takeaway
Opposites are defined by the impossibility of their coexistence in the same subject at the same time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a pair of contradictory beliefs you hold about yourself, like 'I am capable' and 'I am worthless'. According to the definition, can they truly coexist in your heart at the same moment? What would it take to remove one of them completely?
?
In your daily life, are there situations where you treat two different things as if they are opposites when they are actually like man and horse? Give a concrete example and explain why this distinction matters for your faith or actions.
?
The definition of opposites relies on the impossibility of coexistence in a single subject. Is your heart a single subject? Then can it simultaneously love Allah and love sin? Confront this directly: where do you stand right now?

Classification of Opposites
Al-Jurjani classifies opposites into four types using a dichotomous schema.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourfold classification of opposites into correlatives, contraries, privation/disposition, and negation/affirmation, and the criteria for each.

Core Idea
Opposites fall into four categories based on existentiality and relationality.

After establishing the definition of opposites, al-Jurjani presents the well-known classification that divides opposites based on whether both are existential or one is privative, and further according to how they are conceived. This classification is a standard tool in logic and kalam for analyzing opposed concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first division
Notice that the classification first divides opposites into those where both are existential (positive) and those where one is existential and the other privative.
'Existential' here means the concept does not involve negation.
2
Understand correlatives vs. contraries
Within existential opposites, if each is conceived in relation to the other, they are correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship). If not, they are contraries (e.g., whiteness and blackness).
Correlatives imply each other; contraries do not require mutual implication.
3
Learn the mixed case: privation/disposition vs. negation/affirmation
When one opposite is existential and the other privative, check whether the privative considers a subject capable of the existential. If yes, it's privation/disposition (e.g., sight and blindness). If no, it's negation/affirmation (e.g., whiteness and non-whiteness).
The key is whether the privative term implies a potential for the existential attribute.
4
Review the gloss on 'existential'
The gloss clarifies that 'existential' means negation is not part of the concept of either term, which helps distinguish the two main branches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: (existential) i.e., negation is not included in the concept of either of them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all opposites are symmetrical pairs like white/black.
Correction: Some opposites involve privation (absence) and require a subject capable of the positive attribute, e.g., blindness/sight. Others are simple negations, like non-white/white.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of correlatives (mutadayifan)?
A) Whiteness and blackness
B) Sight and blindness
C) Fatherhood and sonship
D) Whiteness and non-whiteness

Key Takeaway
Opposites are classified based on whether both are existential and how they relate, yielding four distinct logical categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that some opposites are merely the presence and absence of a quality in a subject, like sight and blindness. When you consider your own spiritual sight — your ability to recognize truth from falsehood — what 'blindness' in your heart might you be ignoring? What would it take for you to honestly acknowledge that absence and seek to restore the disposition?
?
The classification of opposites is not just an academic exercise; it shapes how you understand Allah's names and attributes. Is there an attribute of Allah that you have inadvertently reduced to a mere negation (e.g., 'Allah is not ignorant') rather than affirming it as a positive, existential perfection? How might that affect your reverence for Him?
?
If you truly grasped that some opposites are correlatives — each defined by the other — how would that change the way you view your relationship with your Creator? Are you content to see yourself only in relation to creation, or will you see your very existence as correlative to His lordship?

Objection and Answer on Privation Opposites
Al-Jurjani rebuts the objection that both opposites could be privations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two pure privations cannot be true opposites and how al-Jurjani defends the classification of opposites against the objection of privation pairs.

Core Idea
Two privations can coexist, so they cannot be opposites.

Al-Jurjani addresses a sophisticated objection: why can't both members of an opposite pair be privations? He shows that absolute and relative non-existence do not truly oppose each other, and that genuine opposition requires a single subject and the impossibility of coexistence. This passage sharpens our understanding of the logic of opposites in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the Arabic passage once through. Note the objection about blindness and non-blindness, and the quick answer distinguishing absolute and relative non-existence.
The objection seems plausible at first glance.
2
Distinguish absolute vs relative non-existence
Contrast al-‘adm al-mutlaq (absolute non-existence) with al-‘adm al-muḍāf (relative non-existence). Understand why absolute non-existence cannot oppose itself.
Relative non-existence is the absence of a specific attribute in a subject capable of it.
3
Analyze the blindness example
Break down the two interpretations of 'non-blindness' and how each resolves the alleged opposition.
This shows the subtlety in defining privative terms.
4
Understand the requirement of a single subject
Learn why opposites must be referred to a single subject for genuine opposition. Explore the role of the intermediary (wāṣiṭa).
Without a common subject, no opposition is possible.
5
See how the objection is refuted
Follow how the conditions of genuine opposition rule out the possibility that both opposites are pure privations, and how the glosses are addressed.
Final examples solidify the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض عليه أولاً بجواز كونهما عدميين كالعمى واللاعمى. وأجيب : بأن العدم المطلق لا يقابل نفسه، ولا العدم المضاف؛ لاجتماعه معه، والعدم المضاف لا يقابل العدم المضاف لاجتماعهما في كل موجود مغاير لما أضيف إليه العدمان، وأما العمى فهو انتفاء البصر عما هو قابل له فإن أريد باللاعمى سلب انتفاء البصر، فهو البصر بعينه والتقابل بحاله. وإن أريد سلب القابلية فالتقابل بينهما بالإيجاب والسلب ورد ذلك : قوله : ( لاجتماعهما في كل موجود إلخ ) يعني بد في المتقابلين من نسبتهما إلى محل واحد، حتى يحكم العقل بامتناع اجتماعهما فيه فإن لم يكن بين ملكتي العدمين المضافين واسطة أصلاً بأن يكون كل منهما من الأمور الشاملة كالشيء والممكن العام، أو كلاهما شامل لجميع الموجودات كالقيام بالنفس والقيام بالغير، فلا تقابل بين عدميهما لانتفاء نسبتهما إلى محل واحد وإن كان بينهما واسطة يجتمع العدمان فيه فاندفع الإيراد عليه باللاممكنية واللاشيئية وبعدم القيام بالنفس وعدم القيام بالغير فإنهما عدميان لا يجتمعان في موجود مغاير لما أضيف إليه لعدم الواسطة بين ما أضيفا إليه . وأما ما قاله الشارح قدس سره في حواشي التجريد بأنه يكفي في نفي التقابل بين اللاممكنية واللاشيئية كونهما بحيث لو وجد أحدهما في مفهوم وجد الآخر فيه، ففيه أن فرض وجود مفهوم بينهما محال، فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال، أعني امتناع الاجتماع. وأما إيراد شارح التجريد من أن عدم الحول عما من شأن شخصه أن يكون أحول وعدم قابلية البصر كلاهما مسلوبان عن الجدار فلا يصح قوله : لاجتماعهما في كل موجود مغاير لما أضيف فجوابه: أن التقابل بينهما ليس بالذات بل باعتبار استلزام الحول وجود البصر، فهما خارجان عن تعريف المتقابلين.
And it was objected firstly to the possibility of both being privations, like blindness and non-blindness. And it was answered: that absolute non-existence does not oppose itself, nor does relative non-existence; because it coexists with it, and relative non-existence does not oppose relative non-existence because they coexist in every existent different from that to which the two non-existences are added. As for blindness, it is the absence of sight from that which is capable of it. If non-blindness is intended as the negation of the absence of sight, then it is sight itself, and the opposition remains. If it is intended as the negation of capacity, then the opposition between them is by affirmation and negation. And that was refuted: His saying: (for their coexistence in every existent, etc.) means that in opposites it is necessary to refer them to a single subject, so that the intellect judges the impossibility of their coexistence in it. If there is no intermediary at all between the two dispositions of the two relative non-existences, such that each is among universal things like thing and possible in general, or both include all existents like self-standing and other-standing, then there is no opposition between their non-existences due to the lack of their relation to a single subject. If there is an intermediary, the two non-existences coexist in it, thus the objection regarding impossibility and non-possibility is refuted, and regarding the absence of self-standing and absence of other-standing, for they are two non-existences that do not coexist in an existent different from that to which they are added due to the lack of an intermediary between what they are added to. As for what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) said in the glosses of al-Tajrid: that it suffices in negating opposition between impossibility and non-possibility that they are such that if one is found in a concept, the other is found in it; but there is that assuming the existence of a concept between them is impossible, so it may entail the impossible, namely the impossibility of coexistence. As for the objection of the commentator of al-Tajrid that the absence of squint from that which by nature can be squint-eyed and the absence of sight capacity are both negated of a wall, so his saying: 'for their coexistence in every existent different from that to which they are added' is invalid; the response is that the opposition between them is not essential but by virtue of the entailment of squint for the existence of sight, so they are outside the definition of opposites.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the classification of opposites and the author's detailed response. The objection states that both opposites could be privations, as in the case of blindness and non-blindness. The answer begins by distinguishing absolute non-existence (al-‘adm al-mutlaq) from relative non-existence (al-‘adm al-mudaf). Absolute non-existence does not oppose itself because it is a single concept. Relative non-existence does not oppose another relative non-existence because they can coexist in any subject that is different from what each is relative to. For example, blindness and non-blindness: if non-blindness means the negation of the absence of sight, it is actually sight itself, so the opposition is between existence and privation. If it means the negation of capacity, then it is a negation of a capacity, so the opposition becomes affirmation and negation. The author then elaborates: for genuine opposition, the two must be referred to a single subject. If there is no intermediary (wasiṭa) between the dispositions (malkatayn) of the two relative privations—for instance, if both are universal concepts like 'thing' or 'possible'—then there is no opposition because they are not related to a single subject. If there is an intermediary, the two privations coexist in it, so the objection is refuted. The commentator's gloss from al-Tajrid is then examined: it suffices to say that if one privation is found in a concept, the other is found in it, but this assumes the existence of a concept that contains both, which is impossible for absolute privations. Finally, the example of squint and sight capacity on a wall is addressed: the opposition between them is not essential but derivative, so they fall outside the definition of opposites.

Key Terms
العدم المطلق
العدم المضاف
الملكة
الواسطة
اللاممكنية
اللاشيئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both opposites can be privations, as in the case of blindness and non-blindness.
Correction: True opposites cannot both be privations because privations can coexist in a subject that lacks both positive attributes, whereas genuine opposites cannot coexist in a single subject.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are the same concept
B) Because they can coexist in a subject different from what they are relative to
C) Because one is always negative and the other positive
D) Because they require a capable subject

Key Takeaway
The opposition between two privations is not genuine because they can coexist in the same subject, unlike true opposites which exclude each other.

The passage insists that genuine opposites must be referred to a single subject and cannot coexist. ?
Al-Jurjani dissects objections with patient logic. When you face a spiritual doubt or objection, do you respond with intellectual clarity first or turn to the heart? Refining Opposition: Blindness and Non-Blindness
Author's correction to classification of opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author reclassified the opposition between blindness and non-blindness from privation/possession to affirmation/negation, and how this correction avoids logical objections.

Core Idea
Not all privations are true privations—some are pure negations when subject capacity is ignored.

In logic, the classification of opposition is crucial for valid reasoning. The standard scheme divides opposition into four types: affirmation/negation, privation/possession, relation, and contrariety. However, some cases blur the lines. The author al-Iji modifies the classification to correctly place cases like blindness and non-blindness, which appear privative but are actually simple negations under his refined definition. This lesson unpacks that correction.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's departure from the standard wording and the ensuing explanation.
Pay attention to the technical terms: 'الوجودي', 'السلب والإيجاب', 'العدم والملكة'.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage states that opposites are either (1) one is not a negation of the other, or (2) one is a negation of the other. Identify which cases fall under each.
The second division includes both affirmation/negation and privation/possession, but with a refined condition.
3
Understand the blindness case
Focus on why blindness and non-blindness are placed under affirmation/negation: because the concept of non-blindness does not include subject capacity, making it a pure negation.
This avoids the objection that privation requires a natural capacity, which non-blindness lacks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعدل المصنف عن المشهور إلى قوله : إما أن لا يكون أحدهما سلباً للآخر، أو يكون؛ تنبيهاً على أن المراد بالوجودي هاهنا ما لا يكون السلب جزء مفهومه، فدخل مثل العمى واللاعمى في القسم الثاني، أعني أن يكون أحد المتقابلين سلباً للآخر، ووجب أن يكون من قبيل السلب والإيجاب؛ لأن مفهوم اللاعمى على الوجه الأعم لم يعتبر فيه قابلية المحل، وأما عدم اللازم مع وجود الملزوم، فقد دخل. قوله : ( فالتقابل بينهما أي بين اللاعمى والعمى بالإيجاب والسلب؛ لأنه في الحقيقة تقابل بين القابلية وسلب القابلية وإن كان بحسب الظاهر بين العدمين. قوله : ( تنبيهاً إلخ) حال من فاعل ضمير ،عدل أي منبهاً، وفيه بيان فائدة لفظ إقامة السلب مقام عدلين، وليس مفعولاً له؛ لأن علة العدول دفع الاعتراضين السابقين، لا التنبيه المذكور.
And the author departed from the well-known to his saying: 'Either one of them is not a negation of the other, or it is;' as an indication that what is meant by 'existent' here is that whose concept does not have negation as a part of it. Thus, cases like blindness and non-blindness enter into the second division—meaning that one of the two opposites is a negation of the other—and must belong to the category of affirmation and negation, because the concept of non-blindness in its more general sense does not take into account the capacity of the subject. As for the non-existence of the necessary concomitant along with the existence of the necessitating thing, it has entered. His saying: ('So the opposition between them' means between non-blindness and blindness is by affirmation and negation, because in reality it is opposition between capacity and negation of capacity, even if in appearance it is between two negations.) His saying: ('as an indication etc.') is a circumstantial accusative from the subject of the verb 'adala, meaning 'indicating'. In it is an explanation of the benefit of the phrase 'placing negation in the position of two equals'; it is not a direct object, because the reason for the departure is to repel the two previous objections, not the mentioned indication.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage explains a key correction made by the author (al-Iji) to the standard classification of opposition in logic. The author changes the usual wording to avoid objections. He says the two opposites either are not negations of each other, or one is a negation of the other. The term 'existent' here is defined narrowly: its concept does not include negation as a part. This allows cases like blindness and non-blindness to fall under the second division (affirmation and negation) because the concept of non-blindness does not imply a subject's capacity. The commentary clarifies that the real opposition between blindness and non-blindness is between capacity and its negation, not between two negations. The author's change is meant to repel earlier objections, not just to indicate something.

The Four Types of Logical Opposition
Enumeration and explanation of the four types of logical opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of logical opposition and how each is distinguished by the mode of existence (external or mental).

Core Idea
Four types of opposition: contrariety, correlation, privation/possession, affirmation/negation.

This passage from a classical logic text enumerates the four fundamental types of opposition. Each type is defined by whether the opposites exist in the external world or only in the mind. Grasping these categories is essential for analyzing logical relationships and avoiding conceptual confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about details. Then read the English translation to get a general sense of the four types.
The passage is part of a larger enumeration starting with 'Third'.
2
Identify each type and its key feature
List the four types: contrariety, correlation, privation/possession, affirmation/negation. For each, note whether the author says the opposites exist externally or mentally.
3
Examine the examples
For each type, look at the example given (e.g., black/white for contrariety, fatherhood/sonship for correlation, sight/blindness for privation/possession). Consider why the author chose these.
The examples clarify abstract definitions.
4
Reflect on the mental vs. external distinction
Contrast affirmation/negation with the other three types. Why are they considered mental? How does this distinction affect logical analysis?
This is the central insight of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : المتقابلان تقابل التضاد كالسواد والبياض يتقابلان باعتبار وجودهما في الخارج مقيساً إلى محل واحد في زمان واحد، فإذا وجد فيه أحدهما امتنع به وجود الآخر، فالمتضادان المذكوران امران موجودان في الخارج، وكذلك المتقابلان تقابل التضايف كالأبوة والبنوة يتقابلان باعتبار وجودهما في الخارج في محل واحد، في زمان واحد من جهة واحدة على مذهب من قال بوجود الإضافات في الخارج. وأما على مذهب من قال بعدمها مطلقاً، فالتقابل بينهما باعتبار اتصاف المحل بهما في الخارج، والمتقابلان تقابل العدم والملكة يكون أحدهما أعني الملكة كالبصر موجوداً خارجيا، فهو بحسب هذا الوجود في المحل يقابل العمى بحسب اتصاف المحل به، وأما الإيجاب والسلب، فهما أمران عقليان واردان على النسبة التي هي عقلية أيضاً، فلا
Third: The two opposites in opposition of contrariety, like blackness and whiteness, are opposed by virtue of their existence in the external world, measured to a single subject in a single time. So if one of them exists in it, the existence of the other is prevented by it. Thus, the two mentioned contraries are two things existing in the external world. And likewise the two opposites in opposition of correlation, like fatherhood and sonship, are opposed by virtue of their existence in the external world in a single subject, in a single time, from a single aspect, according to the opinion of those who affirmed the existence of relations in the external world. But according to the opinion of those who deny them absolutely, the opposition between them is by virtue of the subject's being qualified by them in the external world. And the two opposites in opposition of privation and possession: one of them, namely the possession like sight, is an external existent. So by virtue of this existence in the subject it is opposed to blindness by virtue of the subject's being qualified by it. As for affirmation and negation, they are two mental things that apply to the relational content, which is also mental, so they do not...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four types of logical opposition, providing definitions and examples for each. First, contrariety (taḍādd): two things existing externally in the same subject at the same time, mutually exclusive (e.g., black and white). Second, correlation (taḍāyuf): like fatherhood and sonship, they exist in the external world according to some, or by qualification of the subject according to others. Third, privation/possession (adam wa mulka): one term (possession) is an external existent in a subject, opposed to its privation (e.g., sight vs. blindness). Fourth, affirmation/negation (ījāb wa salb): these are mental concepts applied to the relational content, which is also mental. The passage follows the author's order, starting with contrariety as the third type, then correlation, privation/possession, and finally affirmation/negation.

التضايف

الإيجاب والسلب
المتقابلان

Misconception: All types of opposition involve things that exist in the external world.
Correction: Affirmation and negation are purely mental; they do not exist externally.

Key Takeaway
The four types of opposition are defined by whether the opposites exist in external reality (contrariety, correlation, privation/possession) or only in the mind (affirmation/negation).

Contrariety means two things cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time. Classification of Cause: Complete, Incomplete, Form, and Matter
Al-Jurjani defines cause and divides it, focusing on form and matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani divides cause into complete and incomplete, and how he defines form and matter as two types of incomplete cause that are parts of the effect.

Core Idea
Cause is what a thing needs; it is either complete or incomplete, and form and matter are two types of incomplete cause.

In this passage, al-Jurjani lays the groundwork for understanding causation. He first establishes that every effect needs a cause, then distinguishes between complete and incomplete causes. The focus then narrows to incomplete causes, which can be internal (form or matter) or external. This lesson unpacks these distinctions and clarifies what form and matter mean in this context.

Guided Path
1
Understand basic terms
Read the opening lines where al-Jurjani states that the conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. Note the terms: effect (maʿlūl) and cause (ʿilla).
This establishes the foundational relationship.
2
Distinguish complete vs. incomplete cause
The cause is divided into complete and incomplete. The complete cause is mentioned but not detailed here; focus on the incomplete cause and its subdivisions.
Incomplete cause is the main subject of the passage.
3
Examine the subdivision of incomplete cause
The incomplete cause is either a part of the effect or external. When it is a part, it is further divided into form (if the effect is actual through it) and matter (if the effect is potential through it). Work through the examples: bed and wood.
Pay attention to the objection and response about the sword form.
4
Appreciate the scope of form and matter
Al-Jurjani clarifies that form and matter are not limited to substances but also apply to parts of accidents. Also note that matter has multiple names.
This broadens the applicability of the concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري الشيء ( المحتاج) يسمى (معلولاً، والعلة إما تامة كما سيأتي وإما ناقصة. والناقصة (إما جزء الشيء) الذي هو المعلول ( أو ) أمر ( خارج عنه ، والأول إن كان به الشيء بالفعل كالهيئة للسرير فهو (الصورة) لا يقال صورة السيف قد تحصل في الخشب، مع أن السيف ليس حاصلاً بالفعل؛ لأنا نقول : الصورة السيفية المعينة إذا حصلت بشخصها حصل السيف بالفعل قطعاً، وليست الحاصلة في الخشب عين تلك الصورة، بل فرد آخر من نوعها . وإن كان الشيء به بالقوة، كالخشب له ) أي للسرير ( فهو المادة ) وليس المراد بالعلة الصورية والمادة ما يختص بالجواهر من المادة والصورة الجوهريتين بل ما يعمهما وغيرهما من أجزاء الأعراض التي توجد بها الأعراض، إما بالفعل أو بالقوة. (ولها ) أي للمادة (أسماء) متعددة، باعتبارات
The Fifth Observatory - The First Aim: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. The thing (the needy) is called 'effect' (ma'lūl), and the cause ('illa) is either complete (tāmma) as will come or incomplete (nāqiṣa). And the incomplete is either a part of the thing which is the effect or a matter external to it. The first, if the thing is actual through it, like the form for the bed, then it is 'form' (ṣūra). Do not say: the form of the sword may occur in wood, while the sword is not actual; for we say: the specific sword-form, when it occurs in its individual instance, the sword occurs actually necessarily, and that occurring in wood is not that same form but another individual of its type. And if the thing is potential through it, like the wood for it, i.e., the bed, then it is 'matter' (mādda). And what is meant by formal and material cause is not what is specific to substances from substantial matter and form, but rather what encompasses both and others from the parts of accidents through which accidents exist, either actually or potentially. And it (i.e., matter) has multiple names, depending on considerations.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the concept of a thing's need for another as necessary. It introduces key terminology: the thing that is needed is called an effect (ma'lūl), and that which is needed is called a cause ('illa). The cause is then divided into two types: complete (tāmma) and incomplete (nāqiṣa). The complete cause will be discussed later. The incomplete cause is further subdivided: either it is a part of the effect or it is external to it. The focus then turns to the case where the incomplete cause is a part of the effect. This part is further divided based on whether the effect is actual or potential through that part. If the effect is actual through the part, like the form (hay'a) of a bed makes the bed actual, then that part is called 'form' (ṣūra). The author anticipates an objection: the form of a sword may occur in a piece of wood, yet the sword itself is not actual. The response clarifies that the specific sword-form (ṣūra sayfiyya mu'ayyana) when it occurs in its individual instance (i.e., in iron) necessarily entails the actual existence of the sword. The form occurring in wood is a different individual of the same type, not sufficient to make the sword actual. If the effect is potential through the part, like the wood for a bed is potentially the bed, then that part is called 'matter' (mādda). The author then broadens the scope: the terms 'form' and 'matter' here are not limited to substantial form and matter (which belong to substances) but include any parts of accidents through which accidents exist, whether actually or potentially. Finally, it is mentioned that matter has multiple names depending on different considerations, though these names are not listed here. The passage thus establishes the foundational classification of causes and the technical definitions of form and matter within the kalam framework.

صورة

بالقوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Form and matter only apply to substances (substantial form and matter).
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly states that they also apply to parts of accidents, encompassing both substances and accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary division of the incomplete cause?
A) Complete and incomplete
B) Part of the effect or external to it
C) Form and matter
D) Potential and actual

Key Takeaway
Cause is divided into complete and incomplete; incomplete causes that are part of the effect are form (if actual) or matter (if potential), and these terms apply beyond substances to accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific good habit you want to establish. What is the 'form'—the actualizing structure—that would make it real? Are you using a generic imitation rather than the specific individual form that fits your life? What would it take to shift from potential to actual?
?
The sword-form in wood looks like a sword but is not. In your spiritual life, where are you satisfied with a mere appearance instead of the actual reality? Name one area and commit to providing the proper 'matter' (conditions) so that the true form can become actual.
?
Consider a recurring sin. What potential 'matter' in your heart enables it? What is the specific 'form' of repentance (tawba) that would actualize virtue? Are you settling for a partial repentance that only changes the appearance? What one step can you take today to make the form specific and complete?

Definition of the Complete Cause and Its Necessary Parts
Definition of the complete cause and critique of the necessity of all its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the complete cause and the controversy over whether all its parts must exist.

Core Idea
The complete cause is defined by what has an entry in the effect, but not all parts need coexist.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary on al-Mawaqif explains the complete cause (al-illa al-tamma). Al-Jurjani first defines it in terms of existence and non-existence of its parts, then challenges the idea that every part must actually exist. This raises a subtle but important point about causation in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of complete cause
Start by reading the passage's opening definition: the complete cause must exist, and it has three categories of 'entry' (madkhal).
Notice that the definition includes existence, non-existence, and both.
2
Understand the three categories
Identify the three types of madkhal: (1) entry in existence, (2) entry in non-existence, (3) entry in both. These correspond to parts that must exist, must be absent, or must first exist then cease.
This is the core of the definition.
3
Examine the claim about parts
The author then argues that it is not necessary that every part of the cause exists; this is not a judgment of reason nor a proven proof.
This opposes a possible misunderstanding.
4
Review the grammatical clarification
Finally, note the discussion of pronouns and variant readings. This technical part ensures accurate understanding of the text.
This is a typical sharh technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري
العلة التامة الوجود لا بد أن تكون موجودة أريد به أن ما له مدخل بوجوده، لا بد أن يكون موجوداً، وماله مدخل بعدمه لا بد أن يكون معدوماً، وماله مدخل بوجوده وعدمه لا بد أن يوجد ثم يعدم. هذا معنی وجود العلة التامة، وحصولها المقتضي لوجود المعلول. وأما أنه يجب أن يكون كل واحد من أجزائها موجوداً، فذلك مما لم يحكم به ضرورة العقل ولا قام عليه برهان أيضاً
قوله : (فما قيل من أن العلة التامة للوجود إلخ) لا يخفى أن حاصل ما ذكره أن المراد بوجود العلة التامة حصول الأمور التي لها مدخل في وجود المعلول ولا شك أن العلة التامة للمعدوم أيضاً لا بد أن تكون موجودة بهذا المعنى . فلا وجه وجيهاً للتخصيص بالموجود حينئذ ولا إشارة في ذلك القول إلى خصوصيات تلك الأمور، حتى يوجه التخصيص بأن بعضها إنما يجري في الموجود دون المعدوم، على أن أجزاء العلة التامة لا تنحصر فيما ذكره، إذ المعدوم الذي مدخليته بحسب الذات، كالاتصاف بالأمور الاعتبارية مثلاً خارج عنه .
قوله : ( ما له مدخل لوجوده ضمير وجوده راجع إلى ما الذي هو عبارة عن جزء العلة التامة وقوله : لوجوده صفة لمدخل أي مدخل كائن لوجوده ويصح بحسب المعنى جعله بدلاً من له، وقس عليه نظيريه ولا يظن أن الضمائر راجعة إلى المعلول، فإنه لا يصح. وفي بعض النسخ بوجوده بالباء السببية وكذا في ،نظيريه وهو أظهر .
The Fifth Observatory - The First Objective: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary.
The complete cause of existence must exist. It is intended by this that that which has an entry (madkhal) in its existence must exist, and that which has an entry in its non-existence must be non-existent, and that which has an entry in its existence and non-existence must first exist then cease to exist. This is the meaning of the existence of the complete cause (al-illa al-tamma) and its occurrence necessitating the existence of the effect. As for the claim that each one of its parts must exist, that is something which neither the necessity of reason has judged nor has a proof (burhan) been established for it.
His saying (the statement that the complete cause of existence, etc.): it is not hidden that the gist of what he mentioned is that what is meant by the existence of the complete cause is the occurrence of those things which have an entry in the existence of the effect, and there is no doubt that the complete cause of the non-existent also must exist in this sense. So there is no valid basis for specifying it with the existent at that time, nor is there any indication in that statement to the particularities of those things, such that the specification could be directed by saying that some of them occur only in the existent and not in the non-existent, given that the parts of the complete cause are not limited to what he mentioned, since the non-existent whose entry is according to essence, like being characterized by relational matters, for example, is outside of it.
His saying: (that which has an entry for its existence: the pronoun 'its existence' refers to 'that which' is a part of the complete cause, and his saying 'for its existence' is an attribute of 'entry', i.e., an entry existing for its existence. It is valid, according to meaning, to make it a substitute for 'for it', and similarly for its two parallels. And do not think that the pronouns refer to the effect, for that is not correct. In some copies it is 'by its existence' with the preposition 'ba' indicating causality, and likewise in its two parallels, and it is more apparent.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the complete cause (al-illa al-tamma). The commentator first presents the definition: the complete cause of existence must exist, meaning that whatever has an entry (madkhal) in the existence of the effect must exist, whatever has an entry in its non-existence must be non-existent, and whatever has an entry in its existence and non-existence must first exist and then cease to exist. This is the meaning of the existence of the complete cause. Then he argues that it is not necessary that every part of the cause actually exists; this is not judged by the necessity of reason nor proven by proof. He then explains the lemma 'the statement that the complete cause of existence etc.' by clarifying that the complete cause is defined by the occurrence of things that have an entry in the existence of the effect, and even the complete cause of the non-existent must exist in this sense. Therefore, there is no valid basis to specify it with the existent only. He also explains the lemma 'that which has an entry for its existence' by discussing the pronoun reference and the grammatical construction, noting variant readings.

مدخل
وجود
عدم
بديهة
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All parts of the complete cause must exist simultaneously.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that this is not a rational necessity nor proven by proof. Only parts with an entry in existence must exist; others may be absent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is NOT necessarily required for the complete cause?
A) That each part exists
B) That the effect exists
C) That the cause is known by reason
D) That the cause is proven by proof

Key Takeaway
The complete cause is defined by the presence or absence of its parts that have an entry in the effect, but it is not necessary that all such parts coexist.

?
The passage states that reason's necessity does not require all parts to coexist. The Simple Being: No Multiplicity, No Dual Role
Definition of the simple being and its inability to be both agent and receiver from one aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the truly simple being is and why it cannot be both agent and receiver from one aspect.

Core Idea
The simple being cannot be the source and receiver of the same effect.

This lesson defines the truly simple being, a concept used by philosophers to describe the Necessary Existent. It clarifies that such a being cannot be both an agent and a receiver of the same thing from a single aspect, and contrasts this with the Ash'ari position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the commentator begins with the fourth objective, quotes the philosophers, explains the negation, and addresses the Ash'ari view.
The passage contains both matn and sharh; pay attention to the parentheses which indicate the commentator's own words.
2
Identify the key claim
The core claim is that the truly simple being, like Allah, cannot be both a receiver (قابل) and an agent (فاعل). Ask yourself why this would be problematic if it were possible.
The negation is not absolute but conditional on one aspect.
3
Understand the qualification
The commentator explains that the negation applies only to one thing from one aspect. If two things or two aspects are involved, it is permissible. This nuance is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.
Contrast with the Ash'ari view that God has real attributes additional to His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : البسيط الحقيقي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً قال الحكماء : البسيط الحقيقي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً، كالواجب تعالى، لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً أي لا يكون مصدراً لأثر، وقابلاً له من جهة واحدة، خلافاً للأشاعرة، حيث ذهبوا إلى أن لله تعالى صفات حقيقية زائدة على ذاته وهي صادرة عنه وقائمة به. قوله: (لا تعدد فيه أصلاً لا من حيث الذات، ولا من حيث الصفات والاعتبارات . قوله : (أي لا يكون إلخ) أي ليس المراد عدم كونه فاعلاً وقابلاً مطلقاً، كما يفيده ظاهر المتن، بل بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد من جهة واحدة وأما بالنسبة إلى شيئين، أو إلى شيء واحد من جهتين فجائز لأنه على كلا التقديرين يجوز تقدم كونه مصدراً للقبول، أو الفعل على الآخر، فلا يلزم كون البسيط الحقيقي مصدراً لأثرين بخلاف ما نحن فيه.
The Fourth Objective: The truly simple has no multiplicity at all. The philosophers said: The truly simple has no multiplicity at all, like the Necessary Existent, exalted. It cannot be both a receiver and an agent, i.e., it cannot be a source of an effect and a receiver of it from one aspect, contrary to the Ash'aris, who held that God has real attributes additional to His essence, which proceed from Him and subsist in Him. His statement: 'no multiplicity at all' means neither in essence nor in attributes and considerations. His statement: 'i.e., it cannot be' means the intended meaning is not the absolute negation of its being an agent and a receiver, as the apparent wording suggests, but rather with respect to one thing from one aspect. As for two things, or one thing from two aspects, it is permissible, because in both cases it is possible for its being a source of reception or action to precede the other, so it does not necessitate the simple being being a source of two effects, unlike our case here.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the simple being and clarifies a key doctrinal point. It begins by stating the fourth objective: the truly simple has no multiplicity at all. Then it quotes the philosophers: the simple being, like the Necessary Existent, cannot be both agent and receiver. The commentator explains that this negation is not absolute but relative to a single thing from a single aspect. If two things or two aspects are involved, it is permissible. The passage also mentions the Ash'ari position that God has real attributes, which is contrary to this view. The explanation emphasizes that the simple being cannot be the source of two effects from one aspect, but can be from different aspects.

Key Terms
بسيط حقيقي
قابل
فاعل
واجب تعالى
أشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The simple being cannot be an agent or receiver at all.
Correction: The simple being cannot be both agent and receiver from one aspect, but it can be from different aspects or for different things.

Checkpoint

A) It cannot be either agent or receiver.
B) It cannot be both from one aspect.
C) It cannot be both from any aspect.
D) It can be both if it has attributes.

Key Takeaway
The truly simple being, like Allah, cannot be both the source and receiver of the same effect from a single aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Does it make you more cautious about relying on your own reasoning, or more eager to accept revelation? What practical step will you take today to deepen your reverence for His simplicity?

Definition of Corporeal Power and Its Three Limitations
Sanusi defines corporeal power and lists its three limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of corporeal power according to the sages and the three specific ways in which it is limited.

Core Idea
Corporeal power cannot produce an infinite effect in duration, intensity, or number.

This passage from Sanusi introduces the key concept of corporeal power (al-quwwa al-jismāniyya) as understood by the philosophers. It sets the stage for later discussions about divine power by clarifying what created power cannot do. Before studying the attributes of the Creator, we must understand the limits of creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Note how the sages define corporeal power as a state that does not produce an infinite effect.
The definition is the starting point for the entire discussion.
2
Identify the three limitations
The passage lists three respects in which corporeal power cannot be infinite: duration, intensity, and number. For each, understand what is being denied.
Each limitation is explained with a specific example.
3
Understand why these three
The passage ends by explaining that finiteness and non-finiteness (in the sense of privation) are essential accidents of quantity, which has these three dimensions.
This connects the limitations to the nature of quantity itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الحكماء : القوة الجسمانية أي الحالة في الجسم لا تفيد أثراً غير متناه لا في المدة أي لا تقوى أن تفعل في زمان غير متناه، سواء كان الفعل الصادر عنها واحداً أو متعدداً، ولا في الشدة أي لا تقوى أن تفعل حركة لا تكون حركة أخرى أسرع منها، ولا في العدة أي لا تقوى على فعل عدده غير متناه، سواء كان زمانه متناهياً أو غير متناه، وإنما انحصر لا تناهي القوى بحسب آثارها في هذه الأمور الثلاثة; لأن التناهي واللاتناهي بمعنى عدم الملكة من الأعراض الذاتية الأولية للكمية.
The sages said: The corporeal power (al-quwwa al-jismāniyya), i.e. the state in the body, does not produce an infinite effect, neither in duration (al-mudda), i.e. it has no power to act in infinite time, whether the act emanating from it is one or multiple; nor in intensity (al-shidda), i.e. it has no power to produce a movement than which no other movement is faster; nor in number (al-ʿidda), i.e. it has no power to perform an act whose number is infinite, whether its time is finite or infinite. And the non-finiteness of powers with respect to their effects is confined to these three things, because finiteness and non-finiteness in the sense of privation (ʿadam al-milka) are among the primary essential accidents of quantity (al-kammiyya).
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage defines corporeal power (al-quwwa al-jismāniyya) and states its limitations. It is a definition and classification. The sages (al-ḥukamāʾ) define corporeal power as a state in the body that cannot produce an infinite effect. They then enumerate three respects in which this limitation holds: duration (al-mudda), intensity (al-shidda), and number (al-ʿidda). Each is explained: duration means it cannot act over infinite time, whether the act is single or multiple; intensity means it cannot produce a movement that is faster than any other movement; number means it cannot perform an infinite number of acts, regardless of whether the time for them is finite or infinite. The passage then explains why these three aspects are the only ones: because finiteness and non-finiteness in the sense of privation (i.e., the absence of a power to exceed a limit) are primary essential accidents of quantity. Quantity has these three dimensions – duration (time), intensity (speed), and number (count) – and thus any power's limitation in producing infinite effects must be in terms of these.

Key Terms
القوة الجسمانية
المدة
الشدة
العدة
عدم التناهي
عدم الملكة
الكمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'non-finiteness in intensity' applies to any action's intensity, but it specifically concerns movement and speed.
Correction: The text explicitly says 'movement' (حركة) in the explanation of intensity, so the limitation is about speed, not general intensity.

Checkpoint
According to the sages, into how many respects is the non-finiteness of corporeal power confined?
A) Two: duration and number
B) Three: duration, intensity, and number
C) Four: duration, intensity, number, and place
D) One: only number

Key Takeaway
Corporeal power is inherently limited and cannot produce infinite effects in duration, intensity, or number.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel exhausted or incapable of doing more good deeds, do you view this as a reminder that your corporeal power is inherently limited, or do you simply accept it as inevitable? What would change if you turned to the One whose power knows no bounds in that moment? Make a concrete commitment to call upon Allah's infinite power tomorrow when you feel your own strength falter.
?
In your pursuit of knowledge or worship, are you relying more on your own finite intensity (rushing, exhaustion) or on the barakah (blessing) that comes from the Infinite? Identify one specific habit where you overexert your limited strength instead of seeking divine facilitation, and decide how to change it starting today.
?
Your life has a finite number of breaths and deeds. Does this realization grip you with a sense of urgency to make each action count, or does it paralyze you with the weight of limitation? What is one action you can do today that acknowledges your finitude and yet reaches for the eternal by seeking Allah's pleasure alone?

Defining the Opposite of Forced Motion
Definition and distinction of a key term in motion theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'opposite of forced motion' is defined in Ilm al-Kalam and what types of motion it includes.

Core Idea
Motion from within—whether conscious or not—defines the opposite of forced motion.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a key term in the physics of kalam: the opposite of forced motion. This definition sets the boundaries for what counts as natural versus forced motion, and it distinguishes this term from another related category. Let's read the original text carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the original Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the precision of the wording.
The phrase 'sawa'a kana lishu'ur aw la' is key.
2
Identify the Two Types of Internal Principle
The definition states that the motion proceeds from an internal principle. This principle can either have consciousness or not. Both are included in 'opposite of forced'.
3
Understand the Exclusion
The commentator then excludes another category: the opposite of both voluntary and forced motion together. That category is motion from an internal principle without consciousness.
Notice the difference: the opposite of forced includes both conscious and unconscious internal motions, while the opposite of both voluntary and forced only includes the unconscious one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( المقابل للتحريك القسري وهو ما يكون صادراً عن داخل في المتحرك سواء كان لشعور أو لا واحترز به عن المقابل للإرادي والقسري معاً أعني الصادر عن مبدأ لا شعور فيه داخل في المتحرك .
His statement: '(The opposite of forced motion) and it is that which proceeds from something internal in the moving body, whether with consciousness or not, and he guarded by it against the opposite of voluntary and forced together, namely that which proceeds from a principle without consciousness internal in the moving body.'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a definition. The commentator explains the term 'opposite to forced motion' (المقابل للتحريك القسري). It is defined as motion that originates from an internal source within the moving body, and this source may or may not have consciousness. The purpose of this definition is to distinguish this kind of motion from another category that is opposite to both voluntary and forced motion together, namely that which proceeds from an unconscious principle internal to the moving body. In other words, the 'opposite to forced motion' includes motions like natural motion, while the 'opposite to both' refers to something else (e.g., instinctive motion).

Key Terms
التحريك القسري
المقابل
شعور
مبدأ لا شعور فيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'opposite of forced motion' means only voluntary motion (conscious internal motion).
Correction: The text explicitly says it includes both conscious and unconscious internal motions. The category of only unconscious internal motion is reserved for the 'opposite of voluntary and forced together'.

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of the 'opposite to forced motion'?
A) It is motion from an external principle.
B) It is motion from an internal principle in the moving body, with or without consciousness.
C) It is motion that requires consciousness.
D) It is motion that is opposite to both voluntary and forced motion.

Key Takeaway
The opposite of forced motion is any motion that originates from an internal principle in the moving body, regardless of consciousness, and it is distinct from the narrower category of unconscious internal motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent action you performed that was truly 'from within' – driven by your own conviction and desire. Did it come with consciousness or without? Now think of an action you did merely because of external pressure. According to this lesson, that external pressure would be 'forced motion'. How can you ensure that your obedience to Allah is always from an internal principle, whether conscious or habitual? What specific step will you take today to make one act of worship more internal?
?
The passage distinguishes motions with consciousness from those without. Many of our daily actions – eating, walking, even praying – can become unconscious habits. Reflect on your salah: Are you present with consciousness, or are your movements merely routine? If the latter, what does that say about the state of your heart? Commit to reciting the opening takbir with full awareness in your next prayer.
?
This lesson defines the opposite of forced motion as anything that proceeds from an internal principle. Consider your intentions: Are your charitable deeds, your words of kindness, and your acts of devotion truly proceeding from an internal principle of faith, or are they forced by social expectation? If they are forced, they are not truly yours. What would it cost you to act only from internal sincerity, and are you willing to pay that price?

Causal Priority: A Self-Evident Rational Order
Defines the rational meaning of causal priority and asserts its self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational meaning of causal priority and why it is considered self-evident in kalam.

Core Idea
Causal priority is a rational order, not a temporal one, and it is immediately known.

This passage answers an objection about the concept of priority by causality. It defines essential priority as the mind's judgment that the cause must exist before its effect, and asserts that this concept is self-evident, requiring no further proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: it begins with an answer, then defines, then gives an example, then concludes.
The passage is dense but concise.
2
Identify the definition of essential priority
Find where the author defines 'essential priority' (al-taqaddum al-dhati). Note that it is a rational order, not temporal.
The definition is in the first sentence after 'جواب'.
3
Examine the ring and hand example
Consider why we say 'the cause existed, so the effect existed' but not 'the ring moved, so the hand moved'. This illustrates the irreversibility of causal priority.
The hand is the cause of the ring's movement, so the ring cannot be said to precede the hand.
4
Grasp the self-evidence claim
The author states that both the conception and affirmation of this priority are necessary (daruri). Reflect on what 'self-evident' means in this context.
Self-evident truths require no demonstration; they are immediately known.
5
Reflect on the objection answered
This passage is a response to an objection. Try to infer what the objection might have been: that priority is not a distinct concept?
The author shows that it is a real rational relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالجواب أن يقال: معنى تقدم العلة على معلولها هو أن العقل يجزم بأنها ما لم يتم لها وجود في نفسها لم توجد غيرها، فهذا الترتيب العقلي هو المسمى بالتقدم الذاتي، وهو المصحح لقولنا: كانت العلة فكان المعلول، من غير أن يصح عكس، فإنه لا يقال تحرك الخاتم فتحركت اليد. فبالضرورة هناك معنى يصحح ترتب المعلول على العلة بالفاء، ويمنع من عكسه. فلذلك قال: والتقدم بهذا المعنى تصوره وثبوته للعلة كلاهما ضروري، فلا حاجة بعد هذا التنبيه إلى تصوير واستدلال.
The answer is to say: The meaning of the priority of the cause over its effect is that the mind judges that as long as it does not have existence in itself, it does not bring about another. This rational order is called essential priority, and it is what justifies our saying: the cause existed, so the effect existed, without the converse being valid, for it is not said: the ring moved so the hand moved. Necessarily, there is a meaning that validates the ordering of the effect after the cause with 'fa' and prevents its converse. Therefore he said: Priority in this sense, its conception and its affirmation for the cause, both are necessary, so after this indication there is no need for conception and demonstration.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is the answer to an objection that questioned whether priority by causality is a distinct concept. The author defines priority as the rational judgment that the cause must have existence in itself before it can produce the effect. This is called 'essential priority' (al-taqaddum al-dhati). It justifies statements like 'the cause existed, so the effect existed' but not the reverse—you cannot say 'the ring moved, so the hand moved' because the hand's movement is the cause of the ring's movement, not vice versa. The author emphasizes that this rational ordering is self-evident (daruri); both its conception and its affirmation are immediate and require no proof. Thus the initial objection that priority must be something else is refuted, and no further demonstration is needed.

Correction: The text shows it is a rational order, not necessarily temporal.

D) The spatial proximity of cause and effect.

Key Takeaway

In your daily choices, do you simply assume cause and effect, or do you actively reflect on the rational order underlying your decisions? Al-Baqillani's Definition of Cause and Effect
Al-Jurjani presents al-Baqillani's definition and explains its wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's definition of cause (ʿilla) and effect, including what it excludes and includes, and the meaning of rational necessitation.

Core Idea
Cause is an attribute that rationally necessitates a ruling for its locus.

Al-Āmidī introduces this section by noting that although he considers the doctrine of states refuted, those who believe in it may need this definition. He presents al-Bāqillānī's definition as the best. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the structure: first a justification, then the definition, then explanation.
2
Identify the definition
Locate al-Bāqillānī's definition: 'al-ʿilla ṣifa tūjibu li-maḥallihā ḥukman'. Note what it excludes (substances) and includes (eternal and originated attributes).
Consider why substances are excluded and what 'attribute' means in this context.
3
Understand rational necessitation
Read the explanation of 'ījāb' as rational concomitance (luzūm ʿaqlī). Understand the phrase 'it existed, so it existed' and the direction of dependence.
The necessitation is not merely temporal but rational, and it is one-way.

قال الآمدي : إبطال الحال يغني عن النظر فيما يتعلق به، ويتفرع عليه، إلا أنه ربما دعت حاجة بعض الناس إلى معرفة ذلك عند ظنه صحة القول بالأحوال، فلذلك أوردناه تكميلاً للإفادة. وفيه أي في هذا المقصد مسائل ثمان الأولى في تعريفهما، وأقرب ما قيل فيه قول القاضي الباقلاني : العلة صفة توجب لمحلها حكماً، فيخرج بقوله صفة الجواهر، فإنها لا تكون عللاً للأحوال، ويتناول الصفة القديمة كعلم الله تعالى وقدرته فإنهما علتان لعالميته وقادريته، والمحدثة كعلم الواحد منا وقدرته وسواده وبياضه. ومعنى الإيجاب ما يصحح قولنا: وجد فوجد أي ثبت الأمر الذي هو العلة فثبت الأمر الذي هو المعلول، والمراد لزوم المعلول للعلة لزوماً عقلياً مصححاً لترتبه بالفاء عليها دون العكس.
Al-Āmidī said: Refutation of the state (al-ḥāl) suffices for considering what pertains to it and branches from it, except that perhaps some people's need may arise to know that when they suppose the correctness of the doctrine of states; therefore we have presented it as a completion of benefit. And in it, i.e., in this section, there are eight questions. The first: on their definition, and the closest thing said about it is the statement of al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī: The cause (al-ʿilla) is an attribute that necessitates for its locus a ruling (ḥukm). By his saying 'attribute' he excludes substances (jawāhir), for they are not causes of states (aḥwāl). And it includes the eternal attribute, like God's knowledge and power, for they are causes of His being knowing and powerful, and the originated attribute, like the knowledge and power of one of us, and his blackness and whiteness. And the meaning of necessity (ījāb) is what validates our saying: it existed, so it existed, i.e., the thing that is the cause is established, so the thing that is the effect is established. And what is intended is the necessary concomitance of the effect to the cause, a rational necessitation (luzūm ʿaqlī) that validates its ordering with the letter 'fa' (so) upon it, without the reverse.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage introduces the tenth section on cause and effect according to the doctrine of states (aḥwāl). It begins with a justification from al-Āmidī: although he considers the doctrine of states refutable, some may believe in it, so the discussion is included for completeness. Then it announces eight questions, the first being the definition of cause and effect. The best definition is from al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī: cause is an attribute that necessitates for its locus a ruling. This definition excludes substances (jawāhir) because they do not cause states. It includes both eternal attributes (like God's knowledge causing the state of being knowing) and originated attributes (like a person's knowledge causing the state of being knowing). The meaning of 'necessitates' is validated by saying 'it existed, so it existed'—the cause is established, then the effect is established. The intended meaning is a rational necessary concomitance (luzūm ʿaqlī) that allows ordering with 'fa' (so) in sequence, but not the reverse.

Al-Bāqillānī's Definition of Cause
Al-Bāqillānī defines cause as an attribute that necessitates a judgment for its subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Bāqillānī's definition of cause (al-‘illa) as an attribute that necessitates a judgment for its subject, and the key implications of this definition.

Core Idea
Cause is an attribute that necessitates a judgment, not a substance.

This passage presents the definition of 'cause' (al-‘illa) according to al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī. It clarifies that cause is an attribute (not a substance) that necessitates a judgment for its subject. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping the theory of states (aḥwāl) and the rational basis of causality in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on al-Bāqillānī's precise wording: 'The cause is an attribute that necessitates for its subject a judgment.'
2
Identify the exclusion of substances
Notice that the word 'attribute' (ṣifa) excludes substances (jawāhir). Why are substances not causes? Because they are not attributed with states (aḥwāl).
3
Understand the meaning of necessitation
The term 'necessitates' (tūjib) means rational simultaneity (al-luzūm al-‘aqlī): when the cause is established, the effect is established simultaneously, expressed by the particle 'fa' (so).
This is not temporal precedence but logical co-occurrence.
4
Explore the debate on causality
The passage distinguishes between affirmers and deniers of states (aḥwāl). Affirmers hold that attributes cause judgments without condition; deniers reject causality entirely, attributing all to God directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد العاشر : في بيان العلة والمعلول. (وفيه) مسائل ثمان (الأولى) : في تعريفهما، وأقرب ما قيل فيه قول القاضي الباقلاني : ( العلة صفة توجب لمحلها حكماً، فيخرج) بقوله صفة ( الجواهر)، فإنها لا تكون عللاً للأحوال، ويتناول الصفة القديمة كعلم الله تعالى وقدرته فإنهما علتان لعالميته وقادريته. The fifth observation - the tenth topic: On the explanation of cause and effect. (And in it) are eight issues (the first): On their definition, and the closest thing said about it is the statement of al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī: 'The cause is an attribute that necessitates for its subject a judgment.' So by his statement 'attribute' he excludes substances, for they are not causes for states (aḥwāl). And it includes the eternal attribute like Allah's knowledge and power, for they are two causes for His being knowing and powerful. And the originated attribute like the knowledge of an individual among us, and his power, his blackness, and his whiteness. And the meaning of necessitation is what validates our statement: 'It came to be, so it came to be,' i.e., the thing that is the cause is established, so the thing that is the effect is established. And the intended meaning is the simultaneousness of the effect with the cause in a rational simultaneousness that validates its being ordered by the 'so' (فاء) upon it without the reverse. For the affirmers of states say that the meanings necessitate judgments in their loci, and they are according to them the causes of those judgments, and their necessitation of them does not depend on a condition, as will come. And the deniers of states among the Ash'arites do not speak of cause and effect at all, for all existents according to them are dependent on Allah directly without obligation. And the affirmers of states among them agree with them in this. And his statement 'for its subject' indicates that the judgment of the attribute does not extend beyond the subject, so knowledge, power, and will do not necessitate for the known, the willed, and the determined a judgment because they are not subsisting in them. And upon this definition that has been mentioned for the cause, the effect is the judgment that
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'cause' (al-‘illa) and 'effect' (al-ma‘lūl) within the framework of the theory of states (aḥwāl). It begins by stating that the closest definition is that of al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī: 'The cause is an attribute that necessitates a judgment for its subject.' The term 'attribute' excludes substances (jawahir), because substances are not causes for states. The definition includes both eternal attributes (like Allah's knowledge and power, which cause the states of being knowing and powerful) and originated attributes (like a human's knowledge, power, blackness, whiteness). The word 'necessitates' (tūjib) means that when the cause is established, the effect is established simultaneously with a rational simultaneity, expressed by the particle 'so' (fa) in 'so it came to be.' This implies a causal nexus that is rational and not merely temporal succession. The passage then distinguishes two groups: those who affirm states (muthbitū al-aḥwāl) hold that attributes cause judgments in their loci without condition; the deniers of states (nufāt al-aḥwāl) among the Ash‘arites reject causality altogether, attributing all existents directly to God without intermediate causality. However, even the proponents of states among the Ash‘arites agree with the deniers that existents are directly from God, because states are not existents. Finally, the definition's phrase 'for its subject' (li-maḥallihā) shows that the judgment (ḥukm) of the attribute does not extend beyond the subject itself; for example, knowledge does not impose a judgment on the known object, because the known is not a subject of the attribute. Based on this definition, the effect (al-ma‘lūl) is the judgment that results from the cause.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cause and effect involve temporal precedence (the cause comes before the effect in time).
Correction: This passage clarifies that necessitation (ījāb) means rational simultaneity (al-luzūm al-‘aqlī), not temporal priority. The effect is simultaneous with the cause in a logical sense.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bāqillānī's definition, what does the word 'attribute' (ṣifa) exclude from being a cause?
A) Eternal attributes
B) Originated attributes
C) Substances (jawāhir)
D) States (aḥwāl)

Key Takeaway
Al-Bāqillānī's definition of cause is that it is an attribute that rationally necessitates a judgment for its subject, excluding substances and including both eternal and originated attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own attributes—your knowledge, your strength, your desires—do they truly necessitate judgments in you? For example, does your knowledge of Allah's greatness necessitate humility in your heart? If not, what does that say about the reality of your knowledge as a cause?
?
The passage teaches that even those who affirm causality agree that all existence depends directly on Allah without necessity. If your good deeds are entirely from Allah's creation, does that make you more grateful or more careless? How will you consciously acknowledge His direct creation in your actions today?
