Two Definitions of Cause and the Problem of Circularity
Two definitions of cause critiqued for circularity, with a defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of cause in Islamic theology and the circularity objection raised against them, as well as the defense that they are nominal definitions.

Core Idea
Definitions that use the term being defined can be circular, but nominal definitions escape the problem.

The commentator begins by presenting two common definitions of 'cause' in the context of Islamic theology. He then critiques both for being circular, since they rely on the term 'effect' which itself presupposes 'cause'. Finally, he offers a defense: these are not true logical definitions but nominal ones, based on common understanding, and thus circularity does not apply. This passage teaches an important lesson about the nature of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: definitions, critique, defense.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage presents two definitions of cause (علة). Find each one and note their wording. The first is in terms of necessitating an effect immediately; the second is in terms of being that by which something is caused.
3
Understand the circularity critique
For each definition, the commentator shows how it is circular because the term 'effect' or 'caused' depends on the concept of cause. Trace the argument step by step.
Circularity (دور) occurs when definition and defined term rely on each other.
4
Examine the defense
The commentator provides an answer: these definitions are nominal (رسمي), not logical. Nominal definitions are based on common knowledge and are not intended to be logically rigorous, so circularity is avoided.
Nominal definitions are acceptable in everyday usage.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction between logical and nominal definitions matters in theological discourse. How does this affect the way we define key terms?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد العاشر : في بيان العلة والمعلول. وأما نحو قولهم : العلة ما توجب معلولها عقيبها بالاتصال إذا لم يمنع منه مانع . أو العلة ما كان المعتل به معللا . فدوري . أما الأول، فلأن المعلول مشتق من العلة إذ معناه ماله علة، فتتوقف معرفته على معرفتها، فلزم الدور. وأما الثاني فلأنه عرف العلة بالمعتل والمعلل، ومعرفة كل منهما موقوفة على معرفة العلة فالدور لازم. وقد أجيب عنه : بأن تعريف العلة الاصطلاحية بما علم عرفاً أنه معلول ليس من الدور في شيء فيكون هذا تعريفاً رسمياً للعلة.
The Tenth Goal: On Explaining Cause and Effect. As for their statement: The cause is that which necessitates its effect immediately upon contact if no preventer prevents. Or: The cause is that by which the effect is caused. So it is circular. As for the first, because 'effect' is derived from 'cause' since its meaning is 'that which has a cause,' so knowledge of it depends on knowledge of the cause, thus circularity follows. As for the second, because he defined cause by 'the caused' and 'the causative,' and knowledge of each depends on knowledge of the cause, so circularity is necessary. And it was answered: That defining the technical cause by what is commonly known as 'effect' is not circular at all, for this is a nominal definition of the cause.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents two definitions of 'cause' (ʿilla) in Islamic theology and critiques them for circularity. The first definition states that the cause is 'what necessitates its effect immediately if no preventer prevents.' The second defines cause as 'that by which the effect is caused.' The commentator points out that both are circular: the first because 'effect' (maʿlūl) is derived from 'cause,' so knowing the effect requires prior knowledge of the cause; the second because it defines cause using 'the caused' and 'the causative,' again requiring prior knowledge of cause. The commentator then presents a defense: this is only a nominal definition (taʿrīf rasmī) based on common understanding, not a true logical definition, so circularity is avoided.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
دور
تعريف رسمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All circular definitions are inherently invalid.
Correction: Nominal definitions based on common understanding can be useful without being logically circular.

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism of the two definitions of cause presented here?
A) They are too vague.
B) They are circular because they define cause in terms of effect.
C) They violate the principle of non-contradiction.
D) They assume the existence of a preventer.

Key Takeaway
The two definitions of cause are circular if taken as logical definitions, but they are often only nominal definitions based on common usage, which avoids circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your own identity or beliefs, do you ever use circular reasoning (e.g., 'I believe this because I believe it')? How can you break that cycle and seek a deeper cause?
?
The commentator defends nominal definitions as useful. In your life, what labels do you use that are merely nominal—comfortable descriptions that avoid true self-examination? What would a real definition of that aspect of your life reveal?
?
The critique of the first definition shows that effects depend on causes. What are the most significant effects in your life (habits, choices, reactions) whose causes you have not traced? What might you change if you traced them back to their real cause?
